O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by completing it I have reached its end, and through its expositions and declarations, it seems to me that I have fulfilled it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the surgical operation with my hands.
Because the surgical operation is widely neglected in our region and our time, the science of it is greatly endangered, and its practice is lost; and there remain only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have misled many and led to error and hesitation, so that understanding of it is obscured and its strength and art are diminished.
And it seems to me that I will revitalize it [the surgical operation] through the organization of this treatise, in accordance with the method of exposition, declaration, and summarization; and so that it will be made clear through the descriptions of cautery instruments and other tools of the craft, both by adding explanations and by preparing them [the tools], which is necessary.
However, the reason why a skilled artisan with his hands is not easily found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be practiced in the science of anatomy, which includes knowledge of the locations and forms of the body's parts, their connections, and separations;  and must have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and tendons, their numbers and courses; and of the pulsating and still veins, and the locations of their exits.
And therefore Hippocrates said that with a name it is much, but in the work little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said concerning anatomy, cannot be certain of not falling into an error that will cause people to die, as I have seen many such cases. For some are trained in this science but abandon it without experience or knowledge.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled surgeon inadvertently open a scrophulous abscess on a woman's neck, and severed some arteries in her neck so that a great deal of blood flowed, causing the woman to die in his hands.
And I saw another surgeon who claimed to have removed a stone from a man who had suffered from this affliction for a long time, and the stone was large.  He went to the man and removed the stone, taking some of the bladder tissue with it, and the man died on the third day.
And I, having already tried to stop it, saw that because of the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew beforehand that he would die.
And I saw another surgeon who received payment from a duke in our land for surgery, and he went to a blackguard who had a fractured thigh; but because of the surgeon's ignorance, he treated the fracture, placing the wound over pads and splints with excessive tightness, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And he, according to his desires, set it, rejoined it, and left it for a few days, and ordered that the ligament be released.  And it remained like that until the thigh and foot became abscessed and succumbed to ruin.
And I was called to him, and proceeded to release the ligament, and thus tranquility and relief from his pain were achieved.
However, the corruption had already taken hold in the limb, so I could not stop it, and the corruption continued in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it ulcerated for several days, until its malice was magnified with the patient's suffering.
Since cancer is pure melancholy humor, it should not be treated with a total iron cauterization but rather on a limb where it is sufficient to remove the disease.
Therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical procedure be divided into two parts: one, the operation that is associated with health, and the other, the work which carries peril in the most important situations.
And I have already shown this in every part of this book where there is an operation that includes error and fear.  Therefore, it is necessary for you to watch this and leave that behind, so the pages may not find a way to speak and to disgrace you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and with God's protection, and your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best path, through God's grace, leading to health and praiseworthy success, and leave behind the terrible illnesses of difficult cure, and remove from your tools that which makes you fear, so that your work in your faith and your name will not be wasted: for more remains for your glory, and higher in the world and behind your sanctity.
Gualia already said in some of his teachings: Do not treat a serious illness, lest you be called bad doctors".
And I have already divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered from head to foot, describes the types of instruments and cautery irons, and what is necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and piercing, cupping, poultices, the removal of arrows and similar objects, is entirely about the various types of instruments.
The third chapter, on restoring and dislocating, and on treating twists and similar injuries;  ordered from head to foot, and concerning the types of instruments.
It is necessary that I describe the benefits and harms of this cauterization, and in which temperament it is forbidden.
Therefore, I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is long, detailed, scientifically subtle, and secretly hidden; and many learned individuals have already spoken about it, and disagreed, and it suffices for me to take a little from their discussions, fearing to prolong this.
And therefore I say that cauterization is beneficial to all temperaments universally, whether with matter or without matter, except for two temperaments, which are hot temperaments without matter.
In the hot and dry temperament with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cauterization is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cauterization is not suitable for an illness that is due to heat and dryness, since the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inappropriate for a hot and dry illness to be treated with something that is also hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite argue that cauterization is beneficial in all hot and dry illnesses that affect the body, because when you compare the body's moisture to the nature of fire, you find the body to be cold.
And I say, based on this argument, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, this should not be taken up by one who is not experienced and practiced in the chapter on cauterization through prior exercises, and who has not known the diversity of human temperaments and the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and the time-frame of these illnesses.
But in other temperaments, you have no fear, and especially in cold and moist illnesses, for all physicians have agreed upon these, and are not divided on the use of cauterization in them.
And know, children, that the secret of healing through cauterization with fire, and its effectiveness,  is in contrast to cauterization with medicinal burning agents, because fire is simple, its action only affecting the member being cauterized and not harming the adjacent member, except perhaps with little harm.
But the action of medicinal burning agents reaches the members further away, and easily brings illness of difficult healing to the afflicted member and easily causes pain.
But fire, because of its superior nature and beneficial substance, does not do this, but rather it simply burns. And this is known to us through experience and a long study of the art, concerning the truth of the causes; therefore, I am excused from the length of this speech.  And if it were not that this is not found in my book on this subject, concerning the hidden secrets of fire, and the nature of its operation on the body and its expulsion of illness, with a philosophical demonstration, which would be beneficial to your understanding.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time when it is best to perform cauterization, and they consider spring the best time.
And I say that cauterization is appropriate at all times, because the harm caused by the passing of time is overshadowed by the benefit derived from the cauterization, and especially if the cauterization is for severe, sudden pains or necessary ones, which cannot tolerate delay because the feared harm may be greater than the minor harm caused by time passing.
And this is not considered by you, children, or by foolish doctors, because it is understood that the illness cured by cauterization does not have a fixed time for treatment, and they disregard that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they imagine, for cauterization is not simply a place for medicine, but rather an alteration of the constitution, drying out the moisture which is the cause of the pain.
However, cauterization is superior to other medicine because of its power, efficacy, and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that their illness may return at some time, according to the patient's constitution, the location of their illness, the body's disposition, and the accumulation of superfluities within them, as well as what they have ingested in food and similar matters. However, if the illness for which cauterization is administered is a minor one, affecting only a small area with little superfluity and moisture, like a toothache or similar situations, then it's possible the pain will not return, but this happens in small cases.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the final remedy in medicine, and that is a true statement, not as they understand it, for they believe there is no cure that is as effective as cauterization, or with any other means.
And the truth is the opposite; the idea that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is only true when administering treatments for certain illnesses, and those treatments aren't effective.  It is only at the end, as a final measure, that cauterization is applied, and then it can be effective. Therefore, the statement that cauterization is the last resort in medicine, is not true according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said this only because of the purity and superior substance of gold.
And they said that the location of the cauterization doesn't matter, but that's not entirely true, for I have proven it and found that this is only true in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with gold is better than with iron as they say; however, when you cauterize with gold, it's not entirely clear to you, because it melts according to the amount you want, due to the gold's hardness, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you add heat to it, the gold melts, and the craftsman loses some shavings in the process.
Therefore, cauterization with iron is done more easily and more directly to achieve the desired result.
And I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, and I have ordered them from head to foot, so that the seeker can find what they want.
The first chapter.
This cauterization addresses dampness and coldness affecting the brain, which has two causes:  a copious and numerous rheumatic flux, called catarrh, flowing from the head to the eye and ear regions, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat problems, and generally all ailments stemming from coldness, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is as follows:  first, order the patient to be purged with laxative medicine for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and usual response.
Then command him to shave his head, and afterward, place him seated between your hands on a smooth bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
And then, place the blade of your palm near the bridge of his nose, between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that spot with a tent, and then apply the olive cautery, whose form is:
And then place that [cautery] over the spot marked with the tent, in a downward-pressing manner, so that your hand is gradually compressed, and you rotate it.
And then gently lift your hand and observe the spot. If you see any significant amount of bone exposed, about the size of a grain of millet, then lift your hand; and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another of them, if it has cooled, until the bone is visible as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot, and leave it for three days.
Then place a cloth soaked in broth over it and leave it there until the burn scar from the fire is destroyed. And then treat the area with four-drug ointment until it is healed.
And if they say that when the wound remains open, it is better to leave the arm hanging down, because that is better and more helpful.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's substance is burned.
And then remove what has adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery, you should go deep, until it firmly penetrates the bone until the bone breaks, and this is the method of small cautery. And they believe that from that location the vapors of the head escape.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
However, I have not seen these two types of cautery in all cases, but in some individuals according to the method of fear; and, in my opinion, it is better to be safe than sorry.  For the head is weakened by a disruption of its continuity, just as we have observed in other parts of the body, and especially when the patient's head is of a fragile nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and with that usage and method, there is safety.
Second Chapter.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain affects the entire head of a prolonged illness, and remedies such as leeches, cupping, purging, plasters, poultices, and similar applications have been used, and especially if the head has already been cauterized with a cautery that has not benefited the patient, then consider this: if the patient's head is strong in its nature and not fragile, and it suffers from significant cold, then cauterize with another cautery slightly below the previous one. And then cauterize that area above the patient's head with the cautery until the inflammation is broken up. Cut into the inflammation and expose the bone to the extent we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery on the back part of the head, in the area called the *vas* (vein) — which is the area where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will suffer much greater pain from this than from all other head cauteries.
And I will say this about the cautery in its place, for the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized must be a more delicate cautery than the one used on the middle part of the head; and this is the method for it.
Third Chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic migraines.
When there is constant pain in the head, or pain spreading to the eye, the head should be cleansed with remedies, and the other treatment I have mentioned in the discussions of diseases should be administered. If all else fails, cauterization in that area is done in two ways: either with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
However, cauterization with a burning medicine is when you take a clove of garlic, peel it, and excise the ends of both its heads. And then, with a thin lancet, cut into the painful area on the temple until a small area is created underneath the skin, so that it is hidden.
Then press upon it with a compress for a slow constriction, and leave it for three hours; then remove it and take away the garlic, and allow the wound to heal for two or three days, and then place a cloth soaked in wine or something similar on the area until it forms a scab.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron, according to this method: apply fire with a cautery, the procedure for which is as follows:
and it is called a 'claus clavilhs', because the head of it is shaped like a nail, with a slight curvature, and in the middle is a small projection.
And then place it over the painful area, and hold your hand — so that the tissue underneath isn't burned — and slowly rotate the small iron with speed.
And if the amount burned by the application of the cautery is as much as half of it, lift your hand so that the artery underneath isn't burned, so that blood flow doesn't occur.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; then place cotton soaked in wine, and then treat with ointment until healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the blade of a scalpel, according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut an artery in this specific, not-ancient, procedure.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the cauterization of old migraines.
When you treat a migraine using the methods described in the previous treatment, and in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is ineffective, and sometimes because the illness prevents you from using the previous cauterization methods with medicine or with fire, then you should heat the surgical knife until it is white-hot, and then apply it to the area of pain in a line that is half the length of your finger, or less; and hold your hand in place once and press firmly until you cut the artery and penetrate to the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful about the continuation of the jaw, which is strained by chewing, and that the ligament and the nerve that move it are not injured, and that a spasm does not ensue.
And if it concerns the guarding and caution against the flow of blood from the cut artery, for there is a fear in cutting it, and especially for the one who does not know what they are doing and is not skilled and experienced; and abandons the procedure prematurely.
And I will come back to the treatment of accidental blood flow from an artery according to its specific method, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this ailment is not cured by this cauterization, and you see that the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize the middle of the head as previously described, and treat the wound until it heals.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we described in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades; this is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of cauterization for ear pain.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxatives and the other remedies mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one called the 'puncta' cautery, which has this form:
And then apply the cautery, close to the ignition point, and move it around the entire ear in a circular motion, or on the spot where the pain is localized if the pain is in a particular area. Move the cautery along the ear's base slowly, after marking the location with a probe.
And if the cautery has made ten punctures in the ear, and cauterizes those, and then treat the areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Of cauterization for torture.
Torture which is cured with cauterization is only of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of torture with poultices, purges, and remedies, and your treatment doesn't work, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: the first at the base of the ear, the second a little below their temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the diseased part, for softening only occurs in the apparently healthy part.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery at the right point of the ear's extremity, a little above the corner of the head, and another on the temple, its length being equal to the length of the thumb.  You should press your cautery until the amount of the diseased area's thickness is half burned.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgery, the method of which is described below. However, it is more subtle than what you see; and the knife should remove a little bit of superfluous flesh.
And then treat the area with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is longstanding and you have treated it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is not succeeding, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize in four places: above all the corners of their head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery in the groin. And the method of cauterization is done according to what has been previously stated.
And it has already been cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is further back; and subsequently it should be treated with what you are continuing to do.

Of cauterization of forgetfulness caused by flame.
There should be given to the patient at the beginning large jars and pills that purify the brain.

And apply that several times, for that is a method of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And thus, if that heals him well, then do three cauteries on the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head down to the lowest part of the neck. And place the width of a finger between each cautery, and then treat the cautery with what has been previously described.
But if the application [of cauterization] is gentle and the patient tolerates it, then apply the cautery to the middle part; and if the application is more intense, then cauterize it at the two points/horns.

Chapter 9.
Of cauterization for paralysis and softening of the entire body.

And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat the brain of the person at the outset with herbs and the other treatment we have discussed in the division when the illness is severe and tolerates the removal of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate the medicines, then administer soothing remedies and masticatories that treat the brain, for several days beforehand, along with improving the diet.
And then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize the middle of the head with a cautery according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the back of the head, and cauterize on each temple. And if the illness is severe and tolerates it, then cauterize the affected area with the cauteries mentioned, having previously achieved paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
Let the cautery be olive-shaped, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then use a delicate cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humours and a thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries we have described in the case of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him to drink what purifies his brain, according to what has been stated in the division [preceding sections].
Then shave the patient's head, and then apply a properly made trepanation to the skull, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held steady on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and an old sheep's fleece is most suitable. And heat that over a fire with a moderate, measured heat, which the said patient can tolerate when it is applied to him. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head, within the cranium, and leave it until it has cooled.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with the other treatments prescribed for him, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize that area making small punctures according to the patient's tolerance, but keep your hand with a cautery that is suitably adjusted: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then apply that cloth soaked in wool or flaxseed poultice.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of descending water in the ear.
When you have identified the onset of water in the ear, using the signs I described in the previous section, then begin giving the patient drinks that will regulate their head and keep it free from moisture.
And have them sweat in a bath for several days.
Then order the patient to shave their head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize with two cauteries above the two temples if the beginning of the water in both ears is the issue, or on one side if it's just one ear.
Cut with a cautery all the veins and arteries that are below the scalp; and let the cauteries be of such a length as the width of the temples.
And be careful of blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see blood flow from it, stop it with any medicine possible.
And I will come with skill in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution against blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck between the two bones with two cauteries properly positioned.

Chapter 15.
Of cauterization for softening the eyelids of ulcers.
When the eyelid of an ulcer is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its shape:

Chapter 16.
Of cauterizing the eyelid of the eye when the lashes are turned inwards and pierce the eye.
When the lashes of the eye are turned inwards so that they pierce the eye, cauterization in those cases is in two ways: either with cauterization through burning or with a comburent medicine.
If with a cautery of fire, first command the patient to cut their lashes like those who trim them, until they are extended and straightened; and if you pierce it near their origin, then you must bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so they are not aggravated until the lashes have grown back.
Then, once they have grown back and straightened, place the patient's head on your lap, and then carefully touch the eyelids of their eyes with a stylus, making a mark in the shape of a myrtle leaf, starting the mark near the eyelashes.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or plantain poultice on the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize the area you marked, little by little, several times, until the surface of the affected area, which is approximately like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned away.
A sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye now contracted and the hairs of the eye raised towards the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab is removed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if any cause returns to the root of the hair and the eyelid becomes softened, then again perform cautery on that area, just as you did at first.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize them until they are raised to their natural place, are embedded, and the hairs no longer scratch the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you command the patient to pull out the hairs until they are elongated and embedded, and then a leaf-shaped bandage of cloth should be fashioned.
And then take known herbs and living things, or whatever that is, and mix these things well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; and then spread some of this mixture onto the cloth bandage shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place it over one or both eyes; and put a cloth soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head should be on your lap.
And place the finger on the medicine and press it a little, and do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning sensation is there just like a fire.
And so, when he perceives the burning sensation, then remove the medicine and move it with your finger; then when the burning stops, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if the eyelid has already been lifted, as if it were loosened from constriction by fire or incision, for then it is good; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the area where the medicine did not take effect, and do not give up until your procedure is successful and the eye is healed.
And then apply a bandage to it until the burning is completely gone, and then treat it with a definitive ointment or something else until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, after your procedure, to watch with careful diligence that nothing of the medicine gets into the eye, and if after a few days the eyelid is softened and some cause of the hairs gets into the eye, then reapply your medicine to the softened area of the eyelid, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and then continue until it is healed.
And this is the method for treating eye injuries.
And know that human eyes vary in size and shape, and therefore your treatment is tailored accordingly.
And the way to recovery will not be hidden from the one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Of cauterization of fistulas in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula using the methods described in the divisions of illnesses, and your treatment doesn't work, then you must cauterize it in this manner: tell the patient to place their head on your lap, and have a servant hold their head between your hands in such a way that it is not moved or shaken by their head.
And then place a piece of cotton soaked in egg white or marshmallow on their eye.
And then apply the cautery, whose form is:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a bird's feather, on the end with which the cautery is made.
And if desired, let it penetrate to the other end. And if you want, let it be shaped like a probe.
But nonetheless, the concave one is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and extract and dry that [matter]. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which is well-heated, upon it.  Hold the cautery with your hand, a little away from the cautery's hot part, near the bridge of the nose, so that neither your hand nor the patient is disturbed. Then place the cautery on the inflamed area and cauterize it.
And so, if your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary.  Leave it like that for three days, and then place cotton with a bandage on it and apply a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is clean as is appropriate in your chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier practitioners described as follows:  Apply to the location of the fistula and create an opening there; then place within that fissure a fine, thin piece of lead (of which the form is: [description of form is missing from the text]).  Place this amount of molten lead (about 1 ounce); and hold it firmly with your hand, so that the patient is not moved, to prevent the molten lead from reaching the eye. You should place on the patient's eye a cotton swab dipped in egg white or water, because molten lead will adhere to the fistula opening and heal it with remarkable success.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have said about the cautery and its treatment, then it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to make a hole in the nose and to push the fistula back into the nasal passage, returning it to its proper place.
Chapter 18.

Chapter 46.
Of cauterization of twisting.
When in some limbs, due to twisting or impact, pain occurs and that pain persists for a long time, and you do not find a cure with medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be on each limb according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, and the location of the twist and pain.
So if it is healed by the first cautery, good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be punctured from a limb to the limb nearest it, so that it is necessary to continue with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some leprous conditions are greatly helped by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider if the leprosy is superficial and can be cured with what we have said in the division, and do not, therefore, be afraid of the illness spreading throughout the body and affecting its constitution.  If you fear that the corruption will spread, then cauterize the affected area on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, another below it, one on the forehead near the hairline, two at the temples, and one at the back of the neck or nape.
And finally, press the cautery slightly into the bone to create a small impression, so that the discharge of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize on the spleen as required.
And if the leprosy has already spread extensively on the patient and is clearly evident, then you must cauterize the affected areas with the cauteries we described for the head; and with a cautery on the end of the nose, and two cauteries on each cheek, and two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and a large one on the vertebrae near the tailbone, one on the tailbone, two on the hips, one on each hip, two on the groin and one on each groin, two on the thighs, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of each foot; and cauterize the affected areas on all the joints of the fingers and toes, on the heel of each foot, and on the soles of the hands.
Be careful of the nerves located on the heels, so you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the hip bone, another on the opening of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more beneficial and helpful it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cauterization, as they do with lances, because the patient's body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small, depending on the limbs and joints, as is dictated by the specifics of the tools used.
Then treat the cauterization with flour of erbs cooked with honey and other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the flesh slightly. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness that affects the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back, and the nerves that affect that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
However, do not presume upon this unless you are knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the pathways of nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of white spots (leprosy, or a similar skin condition).
When the white spots are old, and none of the medical ointments are helping, then you cauterize the area with a cautery iron, a little at a time according to the severity of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color is gone, and the color of the skin is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils mixed with heated olive oil, and with leaves of sorrel, and with either pigeon blood or swallow blood (one part of each), mixed together.  Then apply a linen cloth to the area and leave it there until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Of the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around it, in the same way as it is twisted (or rolled).
And the wise men say it should be cauterized with the back of the cautery in the middle; but I do not see this, for I fear it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized around it with a circular cautery as we have said, or with many small cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of tumors.
When a tumor is delayed in maturation, and this is due to excess fluids, or due to the patient's age, as they are old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the passage of time, and you want to accelerate the tumor's maturation, then cauterize around it with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten in maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the tumor until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a place lower down, so that the arm's limb is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Of the cauterization of a corrosive substance.
A corrosive substance is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes it as if by a fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if that corrosive substance is in a location suitable for cautery with fire, then you apply cauteries with a few small, and larger ones, according to what is appropriate for the location where the corrosive substance is.
And then cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate the entire corrosion and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply dressings with oil mixed over the cauterized areas until all the scabs are removed and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days the new flesh is healthy where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on the parts that remain corrupted.
And sometimes it is treated with a sharp medicine, for that acts as a substitute for cautery. But cautery with fire is a quicker remedy.
And I have already mentioned the treatment with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore take that when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of ulcers and similar conditions.
Often in the parts beneath the diseased foot, an ailment called an ulcer occurs, a deep, festering wound, which afflicts the foot; and cauterization for these is done in two ways: cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is done by igniting a hollow cautery resembling a small torch, made of iron according to the size needed to cover the entire ulcer, and be composed of thin wires.
Then place this ignited cautery over the ulcer, and then move your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it for three days until it begins to discharge pus. Then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you must apply cauterization with water. Take a cannula of bronze, iron, or vulture bone, and place it over the ulcer.  Pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand steady.  Move the cannula around the ulcer with pressure, pressing the edge of the cannula into the root of the ulcer, ensuring the hot water reaches the base of the ulcer.  Allow the patient to endure the hot water's effect for an hour, then remove it. This will eradicate the ulcer from its roots.
And thus do with those and then the others until you have treated all affected areas on the body.
And then treat those pus after the roots of the ulcers are eradicated with the ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 44.
Of cauterization for tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or a cold fever, or another similar cause, then you should cauterize the affected area with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach with an olive oil cautery.
And the tremor will subside, and the health of the cold illness will be hastened.
Chapter 45.
Of cauterization for white spots/accidents on the body.
When white spots appear on the body, and pustules or sores are formed from corrupted, thick matter; then, as soon as it appears, it is necessary to cauterize the top of each sore with a fine cautery, using a myrtle stick whose tip is heated in fire, or with the roots of a long-rooted herb, or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes a cauterizing agent is applied at the beginning of its expulsion in this way; and if it is not believed, and the excess is broken up through these means, the body heals from it.
However, this must be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Chapter 56.
Concerning the cauterization of an accidental blood flow through an arrow wound.
Many times an arrow wound bleeds profusely from the wound itself, or from an abscess or cauterization of the limb, and similar situations.  It is difficult to stop that bleeding.
Therefore, if this happens to someone, then you should press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your index finger over it, pressing many times until the flow of blood under your finger stops and does not start again.
And then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire, many of them, and blow over them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, and quickly place the cautery over that vein after you have stopped the bleeding with your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding is excised and restricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from those which are being heated in the fire, and thus do not stop doing this one after another until the bleeding is controlled.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, lest further harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods: either with a cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it is not already extracted, because when it is extracted the extremities are damaged and the bleeding is stopped, or by tying it with a thread or ligament in a strong constriction, or by placing medicines on it whose virtue is to stop the bleeding; and they should be pressed with a cushion with a suitable constriction.
However, the one who applies a suitable constriction with a bandage and a tight wrap, or by placing burning substances or similar, will only achieve a delayed effect.
But if this happens to someone and the physician is not present, nor is any medicine available, then one should apply pressure with the index finger on the artery's opening, as we said, and apply great pressure until the blood is stopped from flowing out of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and over the artery it should be sprinkled with extremely cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and one should further consider whether a cautery or medicine is needed.
Of incisions, perforations, cupping, phlebotomy, wounds, and similar conditions.

And all of those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in their case death is a possibility.
And I warn you that this should not happen to you, for sometimes the ways of men bring many diseases. And some of them get angry with their illness, and death is near for them, due to the intensity of their illness, the length of their suffering, and the illness itself signifies the fear of death.
And of those is the one who gives you their possessions and says, "You await recovery," yet their illness is harmful.

First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water that is contained within the heads of children.
Know that this illness often affects children during or shortly after birth, and when the nurse holds the child's head too tightly without gentleness.
And sometimes it arises from a hidden cause that is unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those I saw, death ensued, and therefore, I leave this treatment to others.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was constantly swelling until he could no longer support himself due to the size of his head, and that the moisture continued to increase until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the skin and the bone, or it collects below the bone on the spine.
When the treatment in that case where the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should cut into the middle of the head with a cut lengthwise, and the length of the incision should be about two fingers until the moisture flows out.  This is the method of "spatum.
And therefore, if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, the sign of it is that you see the edges of the head opening up in every direction, and the water is squeezed out when you press it with your hand in different spots, and this isn't hidden from you. You should cut into the middle of the head with three incisions in this manner:
Then remove all the moisture from the incision; then bind the incisions with cloths and bandages; then apply wine and oil to it until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments, and bandage the head evenly; and the patient should eat dry food with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, which considers where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left; and so consider with the incision where the abscess and the fluid collection appear, and make it as best you can.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow and the patient die from that blood loss with the moisture evacuation.
Second Chapter.
When there is a persistent cold or inflammation in the eyes or foot, and previous medical treatments have not been effective, then the final treatment in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you want to incise those arteries where the illness has affected the head, you should first rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then the patient's neck should be supported with some cloth.  Then consider where the vein pulses, and mark the locations of each artery near the ears, as they are hidden in some people.
Then, mark the artery with a probe, and then cut each artery with a sharp incision that penetrates the bone, following the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the blade under the artery and sever it at the incisions on the upper parts.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers' width, for when any vein is incised, the blood is expelled with a pulsating flow, leaping out in frequent spurts.
But if the artery does not appear on the surface, then you must measure from the ear the distance of three fingers, and then mark with a probe and cut through to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is about a wine unit, or more or less, depending on the patient's condition and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then cut through that so that a painful abscess does not develop.
And then compress the wound with lint and bandages of cloth, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When someone experiences an old migraine or a sudden sharp headache, due to acrid humors and a quality in the temples' tissues, or a severe, old headache similar to these, and it is treated with the known medicinal remedies and it does not improve, then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or the cauterization of those, just as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hair that is on the temples; and locate the artery that appears on the temple, because it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden except for a few people by the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply hot water to the area until the artery becomes clearly visible to you. And then you take the instrument, which is shaped like this: And then you gently scrape the area with it until you reach the artery; and then apply it where and extract that in superior portions until you extract that from the membrane and expose it from the tissues under it, on all sides.
But if the artery is thin, then wrap it with the tip of the instrument, and then cut off a portion of it according to the amount that its extremities are stretched and contracted, so there won't be a blood flow, because if it's not cut, the blood won't rise at all.
And then evacuate the blood from one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double flax threads or with long cords, and do not cut it until the wound is consolidated and blood flow occurs.
And then cut away the excess that is between the two ligaments; and do this at that time or another time.
And if you wish, cauterize that with a cautery until the bone, with a knife cautery until its extremities are cut, and it will remain in place from that same work.
And it is better work if the patient does not have a fever or is of a hot constitution; for cautery is of those things that help ending humidities, and therefore is better in youth.
And it is necessary, after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with old cotton, and be placed over it powders well made, and then be treated with dry medicines that make the flesh grow and with poultices until it is healed.
And if, while working, a flow of blood from the artery occurs again, then you must strive to stop that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with cotton, and pressing your hand until the blood is stopped. And if none of those things is present for you, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and apply water of intense cold until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as is necessary.
And the easiest method in extracting an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two knives, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and cauterize each, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between the ends of it is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives should be equal. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives, it will burn each part, and it will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those areas, the heat of the fire is maintained on them, and the flesh is cut off easily.
And this method is better than all other methods and more effortless.
And the space between the two knives should be the width of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of acute and acrid sores of the eyes.
When the eyes are acrid, and the sores are in the veins running on top of the head, and you don't find any medicinal cause for treatment, and sometimes the patient's face turns red and feels like ants crawling on their forehead, and the eyes themselves are weakened, moist to the point their skin is transparent, and the eyelids are irritated by the acidity of the tears, the ancient masters of medicine used a specific treatment for this, and called it "spassa.
And you command the patient to shave the hair from their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, along the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers wide: one incision should be equidistant to the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead; the second, along the length of a bit past the movement of the muscles that are in the lacrimal area; and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the blood vessels that are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And the space of each incision should be the size of three fingers put together.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to absorb the blood.
And then place the spatula with its two sharp ends, of which this is the shape of the incision to be made: which is from the temple area to the second incision. Scrape with that all the flesh that is between the two incisions with the chisel that is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then, remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of this against the flesh attached to the flesh, and the blunt side against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with this all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But the incision should be continued into that part of the flesh, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And once a considerable amount of blood flows, squeeze the places of the solidified blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old cloth, and place on them powders mixed with wine, oil, and sour wine, and take care that it does not become a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of hot or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with bruised oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of sores (or wounds) through purging, and for sores descending from the parts within the head.
When a patient experiences sharp and continuous purging, and the sores on the patient's body are small and emaciated, and the care of those sores is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin is peeling, and there is acute pain in the scalp and subsequent exhaustion, then know through these symptoms that the matter and purging come only from many veins within the head.
And the best treatment in these cases is this: command the patient to shave their forehead, and then make a slit in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin with the slit from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and move your hand away from the lacerations of the temples that are close to the jaw). Until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the gap between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then compress with powders mixed in wine and oil, so that a painful abscess does not develop.
And when you have removed this and see that the abscess has already diminished, then it is necessary to cauterize the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat that with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, just as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and make a poultice from them, and apply it to these wounds to encourage flesh regeneration.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of that which hurts in the ears.
Any ailment in the ears belongs to one of four types: either it is a mineral, or something similar to a mineral, such as iron or glass; or it is from a plant, like a thorn or bean; or it is a flowing cause, like water or sour wine and things like them; or an animal.
And therefore, when a stone or a similar object, not belonging to the ear and not growing within it, enters the ear, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if the stone is moving, then apply a concoction of olive oil or pitch, then extract it, and induce sneezing with a remedy, and hold the nostrils during the sneezing action. Then place some cloths or wool around the ear and pull the ear upwards in parts, for often it is forced out by these means.
And if it is not forced out, then administer extraction with a tool called a *gesti epicicarios subtil*, of which this is the form:
And if it is forced out by the tool, it is good; and if not, then administer extraction with a thin, slightly curved forceps.
And if it is not forced out by that, then make a copper cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the ear hole, and seal the area around the cannula with softened wax and oil so that only the cannula is open. And then extract with your hand with strong pulling, for often with what we have described.
But if it is not forced out in this manner, then take a lighted torch or a similar device with which birds' nests are caught, and place a small amount of it at the end of the ray, and then carefully wrap it around that already placed cloth. And then gently insert that into the ear hole, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then you must attempt to cut it before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the method of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the patient's constitution, and then make that incision between your hands and turn the ear outward, and make a small cut at the base of the ear towards the patient's cheek in the location pressed firmly by the patient. And let the cut be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone, and once the stone is found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; and then close the cut immediately, and treat it until it heals.
And if the cause of the inflammation in the ear is one of the swellings which grow and become inflamed, then administer the cure and extraction of it; but if it does not yield to your treatment, then you shall take a subtle spongy substance according to this method: and administer with that the removal of that swelling that has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it has already been softened by the moisture of the ear, and break it into small pieces, and remove these with tweezers or with a delicate touch, or with a cut as we have described, because the removal will be easier.
But in the case of water that has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing as a remedy, and fill their ear with some old cloth, and lie down on the side that has the water in the ear. If this expels the water, it is good; and if not, then take smooth, round stones the length of the finger, and heat them a little in the fire, and let the patient insert one into the ear canal and place one foot on that side of the body, and strike with another stone on the stone that is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water has been expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water has been expelled. And when the water has thus been expelled, take some paper or linen, and put one end of it into the ear, and draw the other end with fire until the majority of it is burned.
