Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear blockage.
Sometimes children inherit this from their parents' unperforated stomachs, and this sometimes happens in adults, a blockage in the ear canal due to pus or some kind of flesh growing there; and this blockage is sometimes located at the entrance of the ear canal, preventing the passage of sound, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the entrance, preventing the passage of sound, is in the majority of cases more difficult to treat.
And in those cases where sound *does* pass through, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And therefore, if the blockage appears, open it with a very thin instrument, of which this is its shape:
If its extremity has a small width, with one side sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin strands, so that it does not damage the ear.
And therefore, if the blockage is due to flesh grown in the ear or its passage, then remove it with thin forceps, and cut it with forceful ease until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is in the opening of the ear, then take a very thin, sharp reed and slightly heat it with fire, then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have detected a blockage that the patient finds difficult to hear, ease the pressure on it gently, and take care not to injure the ear nerves.
And then place a cloth, according to the width of the hole you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for a few days until you are sure of the wound's healing; or take some [substance], and put that in water and pulverize over it; and use that; and if the ear is already suppurating, then you must remove that [substance] and replace it with another [substance] which you will wrap in a plaster made with warmed oil, until the suppuration subsides; and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use other treatments to stop the bleeding, as described in the distillation treatise.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of [a condition] that afflicts the eyelids.
Afflictions that come to the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes are dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with tweezers or forceps, and separate them from their roots with a spatula, and then apply a poultice to the affected areas.
But if pain with blood flow occurs, then you cauterize those with a thin, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is close to the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that occurs on the eyelids.
Often, in the eyelids, a cause similar to a swelling in its strength and hardness exists, and thus it is called swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid, both below and above.
Therefore, the operation in their case is to consider whether the swelling originates from something visible on the eyelid and is spreading in every direction; and the cause of it is mild.
Then make a slit across it [the swelling], and then carefully scrape it away from all sides until it is drained.  Then suspend it with a forceps and excise it.
And if the figure [the swelling] is large, then you should close it with stitches; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not suffer harm from it, because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the size [of the swelling] is receding towards the inside of the eyelid, closer to the surface, then raise the eyelid, and suspend the swelling with forceps, other than using an incision, and press it from every side.  And if the eyelid is stretched by an incision, that does not matter in any way.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a cyst on the eyelid above the eye.
A cyst is a growth that is generated in the tissues of the eyelid above the eye, and it most often affects children, irritating their eyes and causing them trouble in the morning; and they cannot look at the sunlight because of it, for tears collect in their eyes.
And so you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their eyelids below their eyebrows are wet, which are already covered with inflammation, and the appearance of a cyst is present in the eye; and that when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the cyst inside them.
And a method of operation is that the patient rests their head on your lap, then lay a cloth over them and warm it, and make a circle around the cyst according to the size that contains the entire cyst, then place it over it and press with your fingers so that the cyst is aggregated in the center of the circle, then make a small incision in the middle of that moisture with a sharp instrument, and the incision should not be larger than that made in a bloodletting procedure.
But before that, you must cut through the skin entirely until you reach the cyst, for in the many positions, the cyst proceeds through a fissure, in the form of a white fat.
Then remove it with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers on the outside, and roll it between your right and left hands until it is gone.
And be careful not to press too hard on the incision, so that with the instrument you do not reach the opening and damage it.
However, if the cyst does not appear at the beginning of the incision, but you gently press on the incision until the cyst emerges; then remove it as we have said; then soak cloths in warm water and vinegar, and place them over the area, and compress them with powders.
And some who crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision, so that the remaining moisture is absorbed.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if there is a boil or abscess in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Methods of treating the eye.
When on the eyelids of the eyes there form growths beneath the natural skin, and they become hardened, these affect the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and surrounding tissue, eventually leading to destruction of the eye, which is called "tactinir" of the eye.  It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine, as previously described in the chapter on cautery; or a "tactinir" is treated by incision and suture; or by use of leaches, as I will describe.
Therefore, you should place the patient's head on your lap; then, carefully grasp the eyelid with your left hand, and if it is turned back, it is fine.  If not, insert a needle into the area where a thread is to be placed, under the eyelid.  Push the needle through, thread above.  This should be done near the eyelid's edge, where the hairs grow - close to the hairs. Pull the thread towards the upper portion of the eyelid, and hold it back in place with a tool.
Then, in the inner part of the eyelid, under the hardened skin, carefully cut between the upper and lower tear ducts;  then, remove the thread and place small powders of cotton or cloth under the eyelid.  Then, apply a bandage to the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape of the bandage must be adapted to the height of the eyelid swelling, as this varies from person to person.  Some individuals require a large incision in the eyelid, depending on the extent to which the eyelid is swollen.
And then, use the scalpel along the two lines you have marked, starting from the upper tear duct and extending to the lower tear duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, it should be as wide as the king's finger.
Then place the forceps at one of the two corners of the wound, carefully scraping the entire area.  Next, join the two edges of the wound with fine wool thread or suture, and cleanse the blood.  If you wish, you should apply any excess thread over the eyebrows with some adhesive substance. Otherwise, do not worry about it.
Then leave the stitches of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its adhesion; however, sutures are better.  And therefore, the ancients said this.
But nonetheless, let there be care taken over the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no need for fear.
And there is another method in treating this, and it is that one makes a mark on the eyelid, like a myrtle leaf, and then elevates the eyelid with three forceps, arranged and joined in this fashion:
But if that is not possible, having the forceps, and that is not suitable, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the mark, and insert the other thread close to the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads evenly between your fingers, and then with those threads, elevate your hand with a suitable elevation, elevating the entire area marked as we have described.
And then close the two edges of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with poultices and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes the eyelid's lashes turn inward, then you should use what we have said about incisions in that case; however, a suture in such a case is more appropriate.
To treat with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the figure that is inside according to what I have described; and then, by art, make two thin canes or strips whose length is according to the eyelid's length and whose width is less than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads.
Then gently attach each [thread] to the excess of the eyelid's skin, and tightly bind the canes [or strips] to each part, and leave it for a few days, for the skin will die from the constriction and become useless and decompose until it falls off by itself.
But if it has been delayed, then break it with the forceps, and then treat the wound until it heals.
Thus, when it is soldered, the eyelid is raised and the skin does not prick.
And this is the way of two canes which you will make according to this method: and have the quantity of the length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of lifting pricking hairs in the skin with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and rough, pricking the skin, then the operation on them is that you take a fine needle and place it on a fine, thin piece of linen or silk, and then carefully adjust the ends of it, tying each end very subtly. And the length of the threads should be about the length of a handspan.
And then you should add another fine thread underneath it quickly, and tie the ends of that one if you wish.
And then place the patient's head on your lap, and position it close to the light so your work is clear, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
Then, carefully insert the needle into the root of the attached hair that needs removal, and quickly push it through the eyelid until you pull the unnatural hair out from above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair out above and place it in a container, and there should be one, two, or three hairs, no more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair is pulled out, along with the foreign matter, from the eyelid and away from the natural hair.
However, if only the foreign matter is pulled out and the hair is not removed, you will pull the foreign matter in different directions beneath with the ointment you have prepared until the foreign matter is completely removed from the wound.
And cover it with a bandage, for then you will have removed the foreign matter and the ointment.  Everyone will then press and close the wound, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is too short, then you attach a longer hair from the natural ones, and lift with it, or leave it until it is extended approximately a certain distance, and then you lift that.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a wound that occurs on the eyelid above.
When a wound (laceration) occurs on the eyelid, and it's a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upward, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, and is called a "harelip," and this laceration is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the failure to heal from a wound, incisions, or cauterization, or similar poorly healed ailments.
The method of the operation on it is that you split open that scar tissue and separate the edges of it, and place in between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind them together until they are healed.
And it is not appropriate to use treatments that dry it out or constrict it, for if you do so, the wound will return worse than it ever was before.
Use treatments that soften it, such as fenugreek, and apply it with water in which marshmallow root and linseed are soaked, or diachylon ointment, which is liquefied with some oils, and soak the cloth in that, and heal with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you should perforate it (the wound) with any instrument as long as it is not healed in the same form as it was in the beginning.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing other than a correction of the ulcer that does not return the area to the form it was in before.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of an ulcer that is located on the lower eyelid.
This ulcer, which is located on the lower eyelid, truly is an ulcer; and it is either of natural origin or accidental.
But accidental ulcers are caused by a wound or a cut or a cauterization or other similar causes.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it pass from the tear duct on the left side to the tear duct on the right side, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
And then stretch the flesh outwards with the needle and sew it together with the needle, and cut it with a broad scalpel. And thus, if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, good; but if not, take the thread and place it in the slit, and with it reposition the eyelid, and close the two fissures on the lower eyelid's edge.
And let the ends of the two fissures be two angles of the incision, which you have made until they meet, and let them form an angle where they join, so that the shape is formed by their meeting.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp edge is below the eyelid.
And then bring together the separated parts and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices we have mentioned, until they are healed.
And if a wound needs to be treated by incision, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid, as previously described. And then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have said.
In summary, the treatment of a wound above or below, during the operation, should follow the preparation of the wound, and this has various forms.
And the skilled artisan always prepares in every possible way until he restores the figure to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient according to what is appropriate for him in the operation and with the best tools.
Chapter 15.
Of the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this adhesion occurs in many people, such that the eyelid is glued to the white part of the eye, and is hindered from its natural movement and functions, then you must insert the end of a probe under the eyelid, and lift it up, or extend it with forceps.
And then you cut the adhesion with a sharp, but not overly sharp, blade.  Make the cut small so the patient is not distressed by the operation, and keep the blade from damaging the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should skillfully cut through that adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural shape, and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water into the eye, with which you wash it; or distill in it a solution of zinc dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid and the eye with a warm compress, and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye.  Then, on the third day, use healing substances until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of incision of the nail and the protruding flesh of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, either nervous, and is like a hard, subtle chip; or non-nervous, similar to frozen white moisture, which when iron touches it, or if forcefully picked up with a forceps, is cut off and the forceps does not get stuck in it.
And both types begin from the larger tear duct, gradually extending to cover the eye and preventing light and eye movement.
But the method for the incision is that the patient places their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and now grasp the nail with forceps of little curvature and pull it towards the upper edges. Then take a needle and thread it with a horse's, ox's, or cow's hair, or similar strong thread, or double the tip of the needle little by little and place it in the middle of the nail, penetrating it with the needle, and securing the nail with the thread. Pull it towards the upper edges, and scrape the nail bed, using the hair, that is covering the pupil. And thus you secure it with the hair to the back of it.
And then you cut the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula or small scissors, and leave the tear duct's natural flesh so that the incision does not cause a continuous flow of tears.
Sometimes the nail, with just forceps, or with thread, as we have said, is pulled out, and then scraped with a lion's spatula, of which this is the shape:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and cause a rupture, and that the pupil is pierced.
Then put a little crushed salt or a prepared powder into the wound, and leave it there until the next day; then treat it as needed until it is healed.
And if the nail isn't sensitive and you can't insert the needle, or it isn't embedded in the nail bed, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines and healed as described in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you should open the patient's wound and take a sharp, fine lion's spatula, shaped like this: with subtlety. And if sometimes it is inflamed and congested and penetrates into the tissue, then distil from your flask into the wound a prepared powder, or rose-colored ointment, or crushed salt, and cover the wound until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on that area until everything that is affecting it is removed, otherwise, a hot abscess forms in the wound, in which case you leave it alone and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
Then repeat your operation on that area with reason until it is healed.
If the tear duct swells up in the wound, causing significant harm, it should be suspended with a compress; and you cut it open from one side, but do not continue the incision so that you do not cause a flow of tears.
Then instill into the tear duct rose-colored ointment and prepared powder until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of a boil and of that which is born of inflamed flesh in the wound.
Sometimes in the wounds of some, red flesh grows, and it spreads until it covers the pupil, or it approaches or constricts the eyelids, and tightly presses the eyelids together at the edges, resembling the roughness of a balustrade.
But the operation on him is that the patient rests his head on your lap; then open the wounds of that [affected area] and collect all that flesh with appropriate forceps, and hold it with a pair of forceps or with this; and then cut the flesh from the beginning until everything is finished with the scalpel, and be careful not to damage the wound during your operation.
And your incision should be performed with the scalpel which we have described in the incision of the fingernail, or with small forceps with which the flesh is gathered according to the form that will appear in this chapter.
Therefore, when your incision is complete and all the flesh is removed, then fill the wound with powdered salt, or apply red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a bandage over the wound with egg white until it is safe from a suppurating boil.
And if any cause of suppuration remains and a hot blood clot is present, and you fear a suppurating boil, then leave the wound and treat that boil with whatever will cause it to subside, and then repeat the operation on that [area] until it is healed; and similarly, do the same for any flesh that adheres to the wound of this type.
But nevertheless, you should avoid any treatment of the wound through a natural or accidental path, working with iron, and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that the limb is enduring the operation.
Chapter 18.
Of the collection of pus in the wound.
Pus comes from red veins which are hardened over the wound and prevent its healing, and the wound needs to be treated over a long period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the wound in which there is pus is strong and contains no other illness besides pus, then you collect that pus.
And you command the patient to place their head on your lap, and then restrain those veins with one or two ounces, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of a subtle duplication according to this form:
or two ounces gradually in one place according to this form:
And then gather those veins with subtle force, subtly, and wipe away the blood gradually until you see those veins have shrunk back into the wound and have closed up.
And be careful not to injure the wound with the edges of the spatula; and if your operation is in the middle of the day before the sun, and be very firm in your operation so as not to cut anything except those veins.
And when you have finished, then you instill into the wound either green or red juice so that, with its sharpness, it corrodes what has shrunk back from the flesh.
But if that is not possible for you, gather everything at that time, and then you plaster it with what relieves his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is relieved and the inflammation has subsided.
And then repeat the same procedure until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of a nasal fracture.
When you treat a fractured nose with cautery or a sharp, burning medicine, after the initial treatment has failed, you should not rely on it, but make an incision over the abscess, as it matures.  Remove all moisture and pus until the bone is exposed.
And when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness there, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the shape:
And it is called a "rough head," and it is made of iron.  The head is round, like a grip, and is made pointed by filing or smoothing with a fine file (as in the following illustration).
And place that on the corrupted area of the bone.  Then, rotate it between your fingers, and gently press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this is done several times, then restore the area with drying and astringent substances.
Therefore, if the area is consolidated, good flesh has grown there, the body is free of pus, and it remains for 40 days without re-opening, becoming inflamed, or suffering any accidents, then you know it is healed.
And if not, then it is not a genuine healing, but rather a reduction of the fissure to the shape of a nose, in this manner:  And this is how: You expose the underlying bone with iron or a sharp medicine, and once the bone is exposed, then you take a tribo (a small piece of bone?) in this manner:
Let the tip of the iron be triangular, and the shaft be thin and tapered down to the tip, like this (a description of a shape or tool).
Then place that (the iron/instrument) on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little further away, and then rotate your hand with the tribo until it pierces the entire bone, and the patient feels air escaping when you move your hand.
Therefore, then restore the area with astringent, drying medicines, as I have taught you.
So, when the flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter which had spread outwards returns to the nose, thus making it easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the prominence of the eye socket.
When the eye socket protrudes completely and you do not encounter any harmful or diminishing swelling, then you should leave the patient alone and subsequently follow it, and then apply leeches to the neck without incision, and have them suck well; and then apply a liniment made of atra, aloe, and incense, and sarcocolla to the eye socket, and then press the poultice onto the eye socket with fine powders.
And the method for the powders is to take very fine linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make many of these.
And then place those layers one on top of the other according to the amount the eye socket requires. And then press on those with a firm cloth with strong pressure; and repeat the leech application with suction without incision; and then remove the leeches and leave the eye socket compressed day and night.
But if meanwhile the swelling is softened, then it should be compressed very tightly.
And then remove the compress approximately day and night as we have said.
And if the eye socket has already been reduced, good; but if not, then repeat the plaster with powders, compression, and leeches until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes from the eyelids like a bunch of grapes, and the form of a person is damaged by this, and you wish to incise it, then the operation there is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then place another needle with double thread on the tear duct side, and make that one penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then cut the place with the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea in the circuit of the needle, and hold it firmly.
Then remove the needle and place over the socket wool soaked in egg white, and leave it until the thread with the grape comes out.
And then treat the socket with this, which will comfort it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes, one gathers from all sides with a cloth circle to hold a large protruding swelling in the socket, and then it is pierced with a fine instrument that reaches the front of the socket, and the white-ish moisture runs out, and the socket subsides and immediately goes in.
And then hold it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of alconati.
This illness, called alconati, is nothing more than a fluid that has collected in the socket, similar to descending water, but it is not actually that.
But the method of treatment is that you should have the sick person sit on a prepared and straight seat, then take their head in your two hands and hold it firmly until you see the fluid coming to the lower parts, and then it should be dried and not descend, and the light should be stopped, and the sick person should see the causes just as they were seeing them.
And if it does not descend further, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the fluid is not prepared with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands, and then take a thin, fine spittle and apply it to the conjunctiva, a little bit near the continuation of the conjunctiva with the cornea, in the crown (into the hollow), until the fluid is expelled.
Therefore, when it is expelled, then you pour warm water into the socket in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of water descending from the eye.
We have already described the types of water in the division, and which ones need to be pierced with a lancet, explaining the reason, then according to the health of the patient.
Then you should place the patient between your hands on a bed and firmly compress their eye.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye with the water is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right eye.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right eye.
And then place the tip of the almond close to the eyebrow, across the width of the eye in the white part of the eye near the smaller tear duct, and then press the almond with force and turn your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the distance traveled by the almond be equal to the space from the pupil to the edge of the dark area, which is the brow of the eye; for you will see with that pupil with the vision of the eye through the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the almond into the location where the fluid is, and then pull it down several times, and therefore if the fluid descends immediately, then you see the patient doing so with their eyes immediately, and the almond in their eye.
And then rest it a little; therefore, if the fluid has descended, then you pause again before the almond is discarded.
And thus, when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently rub it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the fluid and wash the eye with it; and then apply to the eye a cloth or bandage or wool soaked in warmed oil and egg white, and bandage the eye with this.
However, we and those of our time use a thin layer of egg white over that wound.
And if you cannot insert your almond into the eye due to its hardness—because some people's eyes are very hard—then you must take the substance called *alberid*, which is of this form:
And then pierce that joint with it, and do not insert your hand into the hole, but rather make a subtle passageway for the almond.
And then insert the almond, as we have said.
And after completing your work, the patient should be prepared in a well-made bedchamber, where they sleep on their back in a dark room, and all movement is prohibited.
And place this on their body so that their nature is soothed, and do not turn their head to either the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain according to its placement until the third day.
And then, remove it from that dark room and take away the distracting sight, show them some things, and then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And you should not do this during the hours of treatment or immediately after the infusion. Having done this, you should remove it because water can easily flow away with great and violent rinsing.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to remove the dressing before the seventh day and treat it with whatever will cause the abscess to yield.
Then, you remove the patient's sight and place a compress on their face over which their vision is directed downwards for several days, while they remain in the dark room.
And then gradually release them from that room and return them to their activities.
And know that in a serious illness, the patient is not excused from occasional observation, and then you will be bold in your approach to the operation.
And it has come to our knowledge that some of those who were at Artach, and some said that a fire was made in the Ayrac, a large hole, through which water was drawn.
However, I myself have not seen anyone in our land who has successfully done this, nor is it written in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is something new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of magdaam [or "medicaments"] so that the one who is ignorant of them might understand them:
And they are made of earth or iron, properly, and their extremities are with this triangular subtlety, the sharp end thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When in the nose diverse growths of flesh arise, some of which have a cause similar to a scorpion's stinger or to a polypus; and this illness is called a polypus because it is similar in appearance to that fish. And from it comes cancerous, ulcerated flesh, of a dark color; and from it comes leonine flesh, not of dark color.
Therefore, in those flesh growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, you should have the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and open the patient's nostrils, and prick those flesh growths with your lancet. And then extract that to the exterior, and then separate what you have grasped of those with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; if any part of it from which excision is possible remains, then you sever that with a thin instrument with ease until no part of it remains. But if it is accompanied by blood or a hot abscess, then you apply what is necessary; or if it is a malignant abscess, then you cauterize it until the blood is removed and all the cavities are cleared.
And then put a sharp, sour substance, vinegar or wine, into the nose. But if the nose is open and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that within it is flesh mixed with excessive pus on the bones of the nose to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, you should introduce a thick piece of lead, and place several pieces, with a distance between each piece of the width of a finger or less.  Bind the patient, and introduce one end of the lead into the nose with a probe, or with any other instrument available.
And after that is done, draw the smell inward until it reaches the nostrils, and it comes out over his throat, for troops of children often do the similar things in schools, and it is an easy task for one who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the lead, one end emerging from the nose and the other remaining inside, and then apply a flesh-removing tool using the pieces of lead, and do this until you see that the flesh is now with the pieces of lead.
Then remove the lead, and place a cauterization tool near the nose, in the area where you removed the blood, and do this for three days or more until the medicine completely corrodes any remaining flesh.
And finally, place a lead tube in the nose for several days until it heals.
But if you need a more potent cure, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument for nasal purging, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small, open lamp, or a small shell, and the opening should be similarly shaped.
And if you wish, make a closed tube like a reed, and the tube of the nasal purging instrument should be flat, open; with a handle at its back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever juices and subtle substances you want.
Chapter 25.
Of the oils applied to the nostrils.
Often oil is applied to the nostrils, and it is used and increased over several days until the condition is significantly improved.
And therefore, you should address it at the beginning of its appearance, and remove everything completely. And then place a cautery or a burning medicine on the area.
However, if its treatment is delayed until it has worsened, consider if it is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a hardened abscess; because often the one who cuts into this abscess will cause great harm to the person it affected.
If the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and incision is possible throughout, then drain the patient and cut into it without fear, and treat the area with what dries and tightens it until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suture of the nose, lips, and ear when the continuity is broken by plague or similar affliction.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, little is gained in that operation, except in skilled hands.
Therefore, when encountering such a cause, consider whether it is fresh plague with its blood, and if so, you should join the two sides of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the broken skin are already separated and each is made whole, then you must scrape each part of their surrounding skin until blood is no longer present. And then join the two edges with sutures, tightening them, and powder them with dragon's blood and crushed herbs. Place a piece of diaphanous plaster, or another consolidating plaster, over the powder. Leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it and replace the plaster.  Leave it until the pus is completely gone, then treat with ointment until healed.
But the method of suturing is that you join the break either with needles, as we have described in suturing the belly, or with pus, as you have been taught.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that come between the two lips in the inner parts.
Often, in the inner parts of their lips, some men develop hard abscesses resembling boils, and some of these are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lips and cut away each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then fill the space with pressed lint until the blood is stopped, and then wash the area with sour wine and treat it with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, there are fleshy growths on the gum, which the ancients called "albulus".  Therefore, you should detach it with forceps or scissors, and cut it at its base, and let the pus or blood run.
And then place pressed lint or other styptic or drying powders on the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again, and cauterize it, for the cautery will not return it.
Chapter 29.
Concerning teeth with a file/iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both on the inner and outer parts and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes black and yellowish or greenish coatings form, which cause a corruption of the gum and make the teeth hard.
Therefore, you must place the patient between your hands, rest their head on your lap, and file the teeth where those coatings and similar things, like grit, appear, until no residue of them remains, and do this to remove the blackness, yellowness, and other discolorations until they are cleaned.
So if you have removed what was on them at the beginning of the filing process, good.  Otherwise, you repeat the filing process on those areas on the next day, and then the second and third, until you arrive at the desired state.
And know that teeth require many filings of various shapes and forms, according to what your operation requires. And therefore, the file used to file the inner part of the tooth is different from the file used to file between the teeth in other ways, and these different types of files should be prepared by you, which you will see shortly.
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You must cure the tooth of its pain with all your skill and be thorough in every eradication, for there is no cause that can compensate for its loss once it is extracted, since it is a valuable substance until it is removed during extraction.
However, when the patient insists on having a tooth extracted, you must be absolutely certain which tooth is causing the pain, for frequently the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted. For we have often seen this from the experience of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which is the painful tooth, and that you know it well?  Then you must make incisions around the tooth with a substance containing some virtue, until it has dissolved the tooth from the gum on every side, and then gently remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little until it is loosened.
And then, if necessary, use large strong pliers for good leverage or firm grip, and place the patient's head between your knees, holding it well so it doesn't move, and then pull the tooth straight, in a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient further pain.
And therefore, if it is not easily extracted, then take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth on all sides with ease, and forcefully remove it, just as you did initially.
However, if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you must cover the crack with cloth and press it with a thin, sharp instrument so that it is not broken when you use pliers to remove it.
And you must carefully make incisions around the gum on all sides, and carefully control your strength so you don't break the tooth, and keep a piece of it, and return to the patient with the injury being worse than the original pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their eagerness and boldness in extracting teeth, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in people that are more difficult to cure, and leave the roots of the teeth all or some of them remaining, or extract them with some of the jawbone, as we have often witnessed.
And after the extraction, rinse the mouth with hot wine or vinegar with salt.
And if blood flows from this, as often happens, then apply a small amount of alum and sprinkle it over the wound; and if that doesn't stop it, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers with which the tooth is initially grasped should be long at the tip, with a short, thick handle, so they don't bend when using them.
And they should be like this, with thick handles until, when you grip them, they don't bend; the tips should be short.
And they should be made of well-forged Indian iron, with straight tips, and on the tips of these are teeth that interlock with one another and grip with strong constriction; and sometimes the tips of these are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, that is, a crane, and they have strong constriction.

Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth that protrude beyond the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural position due to irregular growth, especially in women, and particularly in young women, you must consider: if a tooth is close to another and a filling or ligature is not possible, then remove it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument which has the shape of a pig's snout, made of very sharp Indian iron.
And the incision you make should be gradual over several days, because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that it does not damage the other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And it should be entirely of Indian iron, easily sharpened; and it should have many small points, like the file used to make needles, with which the tooth should be filed gradually over several days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then, finally, line it [the tooth] and remove its roots with some razors.
And if a part of the tooth is broken and the tongue is injured, then you must file it until the roughness of the fracture is removed and smoothed, and made even so that the tongue is not injured and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver filings.
When teeth are damaged by concussion and removal through impact or striking, and the patient cannot hold what is missing, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not worked, then, because they do not decay, gold filings are used in those that are held together, and not silver, for silver turns green and can become rotten in a few days, but gold never changes.
And the filings should be of such fineness and size as to fit between the teeth; and the method of application is to take the filings and place double the amount between two healthy teeth, and then to work them with two twists of the filings between the broken teeth, or if there is one or more, until the substance reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the process until you reach the point where you started, and apply pressure with ease and skill until the tooth is no longer broken.
And make sure your grip with which you hold the filings is firm against the roots of the teeth so that they do not fall out.
Then trim the excess filings with forceps or scissors, and carefully place them in each gap, fitting them and concealing them between the healthy and broken tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus firmly fixed in place.
However, if it is loose or broken, secure it or them with more filings in a similar manner at all times.
And this is the method for teeth and the manner of restoring two healthy teeth and two broken ones, thus connecting them together:
And when it is done, return a tooth or two to its proper position, and they are restored as we have described, and remain fixed.
And only skilled and knowledgeable artisans can do this.
And sometimes, a piece of cow bone is prepared in the shape of a tooth, and placed in the gap where the tooth is missing, secured as we have said, and it remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the ligature of the ligament that affects the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which sometimes lies beneath the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has healed.
And the procedure for it is to open the patient's mouth, their head resting on your lap;  lift their tongue, and then carefully cut that nervous ligament along its length until the tongue is freed from its restriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting there, due to the healing of a wound, then you apply a cautery and sever that along its length until you have cut the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful not to create a tear in the flesh and to avoid cutting too deeply and causing a flow of blood.
And then wash the incision with water or sour wine or cold water;  and then place a linen pad under the tongue;  this is a pad which the patient will hold under their tongue every night to prevent it from re-adhering.
But if a flow of blood occurs, apply powdered vitriol to the area; however, if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the area with a smooth cautery appropriate for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula (a cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes, a boil resembling a small tumor forms under the tongue, hindering its natural function, and it grows large, filling the mouth.
And the treatment for it is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it of a dark color or black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch what is a cancer.
And if it has become whitish, in which there is moisture, then you put a lancet on it, and cut it with a thin knife, and sever it from every part; and if blood comes out during your operation, then you place on it crushed glass, that is, verdigris, until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it has been washed with sharp wine and salt; and treat it with further remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of an abscess in two ears, and of one that is on the throat, and of other abscesses.
When there is an abscess on the throat, meaning glands that are gathered together, which burst and are called two ears, when you treat them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, of dark color, and with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of reddish color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for the flow of blood; first let it ripen, and then either you will lance it, or it will burst on its own.
And if it is of white color, smooth, and its root is subtle, then that is what needs to be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already rested, fully resting, or if it has diminished with any diminution; and then make the sick person sit facing the sun, and their head should be on your lap, and open their mouth, and the attendant should take between their hands, press the patient's tongue down with a very fine instrument, whose shape is this:

After this, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument of this shape:
After this, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument of this shape: and it is similar to forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each one is opposite the other, very sharp, made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a spatula of this shape:  And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one side, cut the other according to this same method of incision, and after the incision, the patient will be cured of the cold.
However, if bleeding occurs, stop it with a decoction of pomegranate bark or myrtle leaf, or similar astringent remedies, until the bleeding is controlled.  Then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the neck or near those two ears, and cut these according to what I have described in the incision of two sides (incisions).
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had developed in her throat, an abscess tending toward a dark color, with little sensation.  It had already constricted her throat, and she was breathing with difficulty, and she had been forbidden food and water, and she was close to death if she had been without treatment for another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had created two channels (or fistulas) before it finally broke through two openings.
Therefore, I treated it surgically, inserting a knife into one of them, and pulled it out, and a large portion was pulled out. Then I incised the opening where I could reach from the other opening, then I opened the patient's mouth and held their tongue down, and then I inserted the knife into the abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was cleared, and she soon began drinking water, and then eating solid food.  So I continued to repeatedly incise the abscess over a long period, and a new abscess would form in place of the previous one, which I incised until it eventually healed.
So I cauterized the woman's throat, which was effective; and then I walked away, not knowing what God had wrought through me.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When a catarrh forms on the uvula, and it is abscessed and protracted, then it is called a perpendicular boil; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a bubo.
However, when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and you do not confirm your cure, and sometimes the already healed abscess is still present and subtle, then you must incise the one that is round and thick; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, dark or black, and it is not in the center, then you should consider the incision, for there is fear of harm to the patient.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, make the patient sit up and press his tongue with an instrument which demonstrates its shape, and then insert a probe into the cavity and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments described in the incision of two boils.
And you must not incise anything except what is properly located on the natural boundary without further additions, for if you incise more than that, you will impair its speech.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two boils, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you must use cauterization without hesitation, and the method of cauterization is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and then take the water described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply a live ember to it, and place it, neither thick nor thin, into the concavity of the instrument, which has this shape:
Let the end of it [the instrument] be concave, like the bowl of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine over the uvula.  The patient should lie on their side so that any saliva or mucus from the mouth flows down the medicine, to prevent any matter from dropping into their throat and irritating it.
And then, hold your hand with the medicine and press it on the uvula for half an hour, until you see it blackened and subdued by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take a piece of cloth and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and immerse the cloth in the medicine.  Insert the spoon with the cloth into a tube or sheath until the cloth adheres to it. Repeat this process until you achieve your desired cauterization of the uvula.  Then allow it to dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Afterwards, clean the area around the uvula with cloth soaked in water, removing any remnants of the medicine.  Wash the area with cool water, and apply external remedies, and internal treatments until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less prone to fear/danger.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cauterization or incision, by fumigation in this way: take balm, hyssop, rue, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, marigold and similar herbs, combine all or some of these in a pot, and submerge them in vinegar, and boil them; the pot must be well sealed, and a hole should be placed in the middle of the pot, over which is placed a concave brass instrument like this; it should be made of silver or brass.
And the end should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula, covering it completely.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry.  Be careful not to use this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, as it would spread the abscess, and you should only do this after the abscess has been properly identified as such.
But if you do not have this instrument, then take a cane and place a hard-boiled egg shell on its end, so the patient's mouth is covered, as the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and of that which adheres to the throat, and of other causes.
Many times thorns or fish bones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Therefore, you must remove whatever of those is apparent, upon which you fix your gaze, since you press the tongue with an instrument in front of a light, so that what is in the throat is revealed to you.
And that which is not apparent and is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for a thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or make the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or have the patient take a piece of dry sea sponge, and bind it tightly with a cloth, and then the patient swallows this, for as it reaches the place where the thorns are, it is expelled. And further, take an eel and drink it with a cloth tied around it, and it be put inside and quickly be taken out; or take a leek and quickly put it in and quickly pull it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this way:
And let it be larger than a fingernail, and at its end be a cavity which you can easily insert into their throat.
And have them raise their head, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so as to avoid causing them discomfort, and with this you push against the bone or thorn, or the physician pushes with their hand.
But rather, let the patient themselves intervene, because of their knowledge of the location of the cause, and push it downwards, and pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all according to the amount which has been prepared for them, until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When a bloodsucker is treated with what we have described in the section on healing with medicines and not by force, then consider the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have pressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have described to you.

Then take a bowl full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth into it, and rinse the mouth with it; and let the patient not swallow anything and hold the water with your hand for an hour, because the bloodsucker, as soon as it feels the water, will detach.

And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and have them eat salty things and herbs, and do not let them drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which the bloodsucker is treated from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, but there is a slight curvature in it. which is placed on the throat, and its tip is like a stork's beak, which is made of leather, and which is sharpened with a file, so that when we grasp any thing, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to the recollection of them, each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncturing and incision in two ways, of which one is due to the nature of the abscess itself; and the other to the presence of moisture.
And the second way is according to the locations in the body where they occur, for the abscess that occurs in the head is different from the abscess that occurs in the buttocks; and the abscess in fleshy areas is different from the abscess in a joint; and for each of them, there is its own judgment for treatment.
And some abscesses should not be opened until the pus has matured within them or until they have reached their full development.
Other abscesses should be lanced when they are inflamed and have not matured to their full potential, such as abscesses near joints. For an abscess near a joint, its development extends until it spreads to the surrounding area, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are easily corrupted, thus causing the contraction of that limb; or if the abscess is near a vital organ, delaying lancing until it matures might damage that vital organ; or if it is near the rectum, then lancing it while it's not fully matured is appropriate.  Do not lance a raw, incompletely developed abscess, but one near the rectum should be lanced before it spreads too far into the rectum and becomes a fistula, or causes lasting damage in that area. However, you must know the time for lancing the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
And you know it is mature when there is a decrease in pain and fever, and a decrease in swelling and throbbing, and a clarity to the abscess head, and the other signs.
And you must know to lance the abscess in the area with the most pus, and coming from the treatment of the swelling.
And always lance along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the limbs or feet, and not near the tendons, nerves, or blood vessels; and otherwise, in all similar areas where there is no reflection; for those that reflect, use lancing according to that specific limb.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave the lancing until the digestion within is confirmed, as we have said; for if you lance it before that, the body's course of the matter within and much impurities are prolonged, and the abscess's edges become hardened and deepen.
But some abscesses are lanced according to the width of the flesh or body, depending on the need, or according to what the limb requires.
And you should administer a small incision for small abscesses, and a large and wide one, or multiple incisions, for large abscesses, depending on their size.
And others are abscesses in which the pus-filled area should be hollowed out and cut open, when done like this and it is at a point where it is already suppurating, like that which occurs in boils and buboes.
And some are incised with a three-angled cut; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the groin.
And some of them are given a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar to those.
But for those that are not like that, for example superficial abscesses, you should make a superficial or flat incision only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, you should pierce it and not try to remove all the pus at once; having removed a portion, close the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and do this repeatedly according to a gradual increase until it is fully drained.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly and decrepit person, because the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient often dies quickly, and you should be cautious in treating them.
Observe this chapter with great caution, and for your incision into these abscesses, you should clean the sores and then consider whether the abscess wound is small, or a simple incision, in which case you apply dressings of lint or old cloth.  But if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the incision are numerous, then you should place medicaments in each opening until they come together.
And if, in the abscess, a portion of the skin has been severed or you have hollowed it out around the edges, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from linen, in the same way as when you have extracted stones or splinters, without moisture; and bind it until the third day.  Then you should continue to treat it and care for it with appropriate remedies until it is healed.
But if you encounter a bleeding during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar to a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding spot repeatedly.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer a regimen and treatment, using the powders mentioned in many places in this book and in the section on [specific methods or divisions within the book].
However, if it is wintertime and the area of the abscess is located near many nerves, then you should infuse powders in warm wine and oil, and place these over the affected areas.
And if it is summertime, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders soaked in water and oil or wine and oil, and all of this should be kept cool until the third day, as we have said.  Then you should drain the abscess, cleanse it, and administer the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary regarding the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses in general; however, according to the method of distinction I have already mentioned, the method of treatment for each type of abscess is explained in separate chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that arise on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and they are often in the form of nodes that contain membranes that are attached to them; and their substances are various.
And some of them contain a sticky, lime-like moisture. And some contain a moisture resembling dust, and similar to those; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in their extraction, there is no fear, as their extraction does not cause a contrary arching.
And the surgical procedure for their incision is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, called a probe, until you know what they contain.
For if what it contains is moisture, then you divide it with an incision lengthwise, by a superficial incision following this form:
And begin the incision at line B to line G.  Then, when the moisture is evacuated, you scrape the skin that contained the moisture, and leave no cause of it, for many times it returns if any cause remains.
Then, soak some cotton in Egyptian ointment, if available, or else in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, for with this you corrode whatever remains of the skin.
And then repeatedly apply Egyptian ointment to the cotton a second and third time, if necessary, until you are confident that no part of the skin remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty lumps, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision shaped like a cross, and place hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your skill in removing the layer that contains it.
But if your efforts encounter resistance, then do as I previously described.

Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses arising from arrow wounds or vein wounds.
When an arrow wound is inflicted, and the clot that forms over it consolidates, many times an abscess results.  And the signs by which you know that inflammation and abscess are from the artery or vein: for when an abscess is from an arrow wound it is usually located further from the surface of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a creaking sound in it.
And that which is from a vein is round and close to the surface of the body.
And incision on this abscess is a mistake, especially in those located under the armpits, in the elbows, in the neck, and many other places on the body; and if it is large, it is necessary to avoid its treatment with iron.  And those on the extremities or the head should be avoided.
However, for those originating from inflammation of the arrow wound's opening, make a lengthwise incision into the clot.  Then widen the incision with forceps and remove the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is exposed.
And then insert a needle and drive it through to the other side, and bind the arrow with a double cord in two places—which I learned in extracting two arrows from the Templars.
And then puncture the area between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood is released, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment to the area where pus has formed until the ligaments fall away; and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the abscess is caused by a cut vein, then you must hold with your hand as much of the abscess as possible with the clot, and then insert the needle below the area you held under your hand, and drive it through; and tie it with a double thread until you extract it from the other side, and then bandage the abscess with a strong bandage, as I have instructed you concerning the grapevine bandage, in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will come out, then insert the thread with the needle below the entire abscess, beneath the incision of the first needle, and tie your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until what is in it comes out, then you cut away the excess clot, and leave what is tied there.
Then place a poultice, which you have soaked in wine and oil, on it, and then administer the treatment that is made with herbs and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess that arises from a twisting of the nerves.

Chapter 52.
Of the treatment of a swelling of the navel.
A swelling of the navel is caused by many things; either by fissures in the abdominal wall, and through these the lining or intestines protrude as in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned, or by trapped air.
However, if it is due to a fissure in the abdominal wall and the protrusion of the lining, then the color of the abscess resembles the color of the body; and it is painless, and often appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location varies greatly from what we have described, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and it returns; and it is very painful there, and it is much larger than the entrance to the bath and causes a lot of suffering.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is painless and does not recede when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it swollen.
And if it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears with a darkening tinge.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the abscess is serious, large and hard, and it is situated according to a certain quantity (or measure).
But if it is due to wind (flatulence), then the touch of it is painless.
And the treatment in that case is to consider if the swelling near the navel is caused by blood from an arrow wound, or a vein, or by flatulence; then you must refrain from treating it, because there is danger and fear in such cases, as I have shown you in the chapter where I discussed abscesses from arrow wounds and vein injuries.
And if the swelling near the navel is caused by the intestine or the mesentery, then you must tell the patient to hold their breath and be standing upright; then gently probe the area around the navel with a probe, and then have them lean between your hands on their elbows; and then circumscribe the area around the navel where you have marked with the probe with a spatula; and then open the pus to a quarter of the abscess away from the navel using a large surgical spoon; and then bind it around with strong thread or gauze in the manner of proper dressing.
And then drain the middle of the open abscess over the ligament and insert your finger into that opening and look for the intestine; and if you find and hold it by the ligament, then you should gently lift and push the intestine back into the belly cavity.
And if you have found the mesentery, then take it with a surgical spoon; and cut away the excess.
But if it is an arrow wound or vein injury, then circle the wound, and then return to your treatment; and take two needles and put strong thread on them, and insert the two needles into the circle you have made around the abscess in the shape of a cross, driving them through; and then gently press the abscess into four sections over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and allow the open wound to heal and close up on its own; or cut away the tissue when it is softened and can be separated, and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the swelling at the navel is of flesh growing there or due to moisture, then you should drain the abscess as we have described, and remove the blood or moisture which you find there, and then treat it with appropriate remedies to make the area heal.
Chapter 53.
Of the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division, the different types of cancer and how it is treated with medicine, and that cutting it out with iron is inadvisable because it would cause an ulcer; and we have described the cancer that originates internally and the precautions to take when treating it.

However, when it is old and severe, it is not advisable to attempt to remove it completely, for I have not been able to cure such a thing nor have I seen anyone else who has been successful in doing so.

It should be in two sharp pieces, long, with a sharp tip, like spittle; but nonetheless, let there be a little bit of shortness in it, so that in the operation with it you do not pierce the intestine and damage it.

And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the left side.

But evacuate only that amount which is appropriate to its potency and according to the patient's condition, as indicated by their pulse strength and constitution.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a cut that extends onto the belly, from the sharp edges of the abdominal wall, so that the intestines from that rupture are [are then] next to some other intestines.
