Of the incisive extremities and the serrations of the bones.
The extremities can be affected either by an internal cause or an external cause, or if your treatment of those afflictions with those medicines does not provide a cure, or if the affliction has moved to a limb that isn't affected by any external cause, then it is necessary for you to cut into that limb where the affliction has spread, so that the patient is spared from death or a worse condition caused by the limb's deterioration.
And the sign of that which afflicts the limb is that the limb turns black, as if fire had already burned it, or there is a spreading blackness throughout the limb causing it to become stiff and to affect the whole body.  Then, cut it with an incision.
And similarly, if the affliction is caused by the sting of venomous creatures, such as scorpions, vipers, or other similar ones.
And if the affliction or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger and do not allow the affliction to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if it reaches the hand, then you cut it at the base of the forearm, and do not allow the affliction to spread to the forearm.
And if instead of that, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the affliction passes beyond the elbow, then in that case, death of the patient is certain; and to administer any other remedy for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do the same for the foot. When the affliction reaches the toe of the foot, then you cut it down to one of the bones (or joints).
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you cut the leg down to the knee joint.
And if the affliction has already reached the knee, then there is no cure but release, and leaving the patient to death.
And the method of cutting or severing the limb is that you should grasp the ligament in the area just below the part you want to cut, and grasp another ligament over the spot.  The attendant should extend a ligament downwards, and extend another ligament upwards.  Then you separate the flesh which lies between the two ligaments, spreading it wide, until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be cut or severed.
And it is necessary to place cloth on all sides so that the area does not touch, and that the patient receives a suitable and carefully applied inflammation/abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while working, then you cauterize the cut area promptly, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your procedure until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man on his foot, this same accident which I and you have recounted, which is that he suffered blackness on his foot from a burn that resembled a fire's combustion.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it affected his entire foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the healing of the limb proceeding with the intensity of the pain and burning he felt, and he incised that foot to the joint, and [the foot] was healed.
For a long time, that same corruption happened to him in the finger of his index hand.
Then he came to me, and I was compelled to counteract that excess with what I put on his hand in the process of purifying his body; and although the excess was not repelled, it began to spread to the other fingers, until it started to affect his entire hand.
And he urged me to cut his hand, but I did not want to do so, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and more than that, I feared the death of his hand from the incision, for the vitality of the patient was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and it came to my attention that that man prepared himself and cut his entire hand, and it was healed.
And I have not presented this account except so that it may be helpful in understanding this illness, and that it may serve as a sign by which it may be recognized and treated according to its nature.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy part of the body, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it collects pus, and then it is ruptured and perforated, and the pus is discharged, and the area remains empty like a vessel, and the skin covering it is like a thin cloth, and it has not progressed to a deeper corruption, and it does not involve the bone, nerve, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged until the corruption affects any part of the body, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to cut away that skin completely, just as it has been rolled up, especially when it is thin and like a cloth, and to make sure that it does not adhere to the affected area due to its corruption.
However, if you suspect that the skin is adhering and that the corruption has not yet reached a deeper layer, and there is a visible opening in the skin, then you treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large and the pus that comes from it has a foul odor, then irrigate it with an Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, sour wine, and vinegar, taken in equal parts, combined in a vessel, and heated over the fire until the medicine becomes reddish and attains the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary of this, dissolve it in water and honey; and irrigate the sinus with that, and constrict the opening, and leave the medicine in place for about two hours.
Then remove that with care; and do this for as many days as necessary until the sinus is cleansed and the odor has disappeared.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, if this medicine is not available to you; and you should take the ashes of wood, put water over it, then strain it and irrigate the sinus with it until you are confident that the sinus is healed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then irrigate with water and honey mixed with crushed vinegar, and irrigate with wine and honey mixed, because honey's property is to cleanse and wash, and wine makes the sinus adhere, especially if the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is healed, then irrigate it with something that promotes the growth of flesh there.
And you should take a diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or irrigate it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so the enema tube cannot be inserted, then widen it little by little with a small iron, and apply a poultice wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the edges together with a suture and leave it a size that allows the enema tube to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus drains is raised, then you should lower it to the lowest part so that the pus drains downwards, because when the pus is confined deep within the sinus, the flesh will not grow there.
And if you cannot lower the sinus to the desired lower bone, then you must prepare the limb with a treatment that allows the pus to flow easily, according to your resources, and that no cause within it is obstructing the pus.
But the one that is over the sinus opening, which remains in place constantly so that what is contained within the sinus may drain. And so that you may know whether the drainage from the limb is profuse or meager, or digested or undigested.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus, if the patient feels pain there, and if there is an abscess there.
And when your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the material discharged over the sinus opening has adhered, or is about to adhere, then you should remove it on the third day, and when you remove the scab, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if a subtle, thin virus is spreading from the sinus, then do not be discouraged by the adherence, for healing is likely to take place; for some individuals have slow tissue regeneration in their wounds, and others have the opposite.
And if undigested pus has been draining from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the sinus' adherence is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying properties of the medicines.  And if the medicines are moist in their substance and virtue, they are made dry, like a diaphanous ointment, when there is superfluous addition of drying agents.
And the final remedy, with which it is healed, is to take diaphanous ointment, dissolve it in rose oil, and add old, well-preserved wine to its substance. Then mix this together and use it.
And similarly in that action, when there is no pus, use diaphanous ointment, or honey, when it has been heated until thickened, and apply it.  Or, take myrrh, aloes, and incense, crush and powder them, and sprinkle them onto heated honey until thickened, and apply this mixture to a cloth and compress it over the sinus;  or apply the thickened honey to a cloth and compress that.
Sometimes, long, thin aristolochia roots and the roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and the flour of eryngo and centaury are administered along with these.  Crush these simple ingredients, sieve them, and sprinkle the powder onto the honey previously mentioned; use this.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the deeper parts of it, and is affecting the bones or other hard parts of the body, or ligaments.  Then treat it with a fistulous cure, as described above.
Of the treatment of whitlow (or felon), and of damaged nails, and of the incision of an affected finger, and of the sectioning (or cutting).
Whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of a finger of the hand or foot, and frequently arises on other fingers.  When it is prolonged, and its cure is less easily achieved, it is either an abscess that boils over, or it is corrupt and discharges pus, until it corrodes the base of the nail, and often corrupts the entire nail.  Often its corruption spreads to the bone, until it has a foul odour, and the end of the finger becomes necrotic, and the colour is blackened.
And therefore, when you have done as we have said in the division and your treatment is not successful, then it is necessary to cut away all the excess nail material with a knife, and then cauterize the wound immediately afterward, for cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is healthy and the nail is sound, and the nail's debris now is pressing on the surrounding flesh, and begins to fester and inflame it, then it is necessary that you insert a thin probe under the nail, to where it is pricking the flesh, and lift it and incise that flesh easily, and apply to what remains of the flesh burning and corrosive medicines until it is all gone; and then treat with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then it is necessary for you to cut the bone and remove it, for a corrupt bone in such a state is never healed.
And if sometimes the corrosion and corruption spread to the finger, then you should cut it at one of its joints according to the likelihood of its recovery, and subsequently treat it until it is healed.
However, if the nail suffers a blow or friction, and intense pain results, then it is necessary to first open up the veins of the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse incision from top to bottom, but be careful not to reach the flesh underneath, for you will cause the patient extreme pain, and it may cause a swelling of excess flesh to form in the area; and afterwards treat that area with whatever alleviates the pain.
However, a swollen finger, which is often found on the hands of some people, is frequently fleshy, and frequently located on the bones, or frequently found on the nailbed.
And some are born at the base of the joint, or the formation of them is in some of the joints of the fingers. However, that which is born in some joints of the finger is not bad, and that which is born in the joints is easily/commonly inflamed/bad.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision is light, and you should cut it near its roots with a broad/flat blade.
But for that which is born at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, beware of excising it.
But for that which is born on a finger near the joints, you must first cut through the flesh with a circular incision down to the bone, then close it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if a thickening occurs on the fingers, either through birth defects, or from a wound's healing, a burn, or similar causes, then the thickening must be removed until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in warm oil to prevent them from healing too quickly, and separate them or place a thin lead sheet between them until they heal properly.
And similarly, if thickening occurs on the fingers with the palm of the hand, then you remove that thickening according to the preparation and the proper form of the limb.
Of incisions of veins, these are varicose veins.
Veins are varicose, which are twisted, large veins full of melancholic superfluities that appear in many parts of the body, but most commonly in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is elevated and completely extracted.
The sectioning of it is done in this way: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, phlegmatic blood in the leg is dissolved.  Then, tightly bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to the area below the knee with a bandage.  Then, cut the vein at one, two, or three points with a wide incision. Next, elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and downwards, until the desired amount of blood has been drawn out, and it will reduce the patient's malady.
And then, tie it up, and command the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are filled, and this is beneficial to the patient.
But the elevation of them is done in this way: you lift the patient's leg, if it is very swollen, and then put it in a bath and soak the leg with hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent. Or, if a bath is unavailable, exercise the limb vigorously until it is warmed.
And then, cut the sinew on the vein's side, either along or behind it, from the knee down, or at the lowest point of the heel.
And then, open the sinew with hooks and scrape the vein on all sides until it is exposed, and this is determined by a reddish color declining to a blackness.
And thus, when you open it, from the sinew you see that white thing which is like a cord.
And then you place a ray (or, possibly, a wedge) under it until it is raised and pulled away from the sinew, and suspend it with a hook or forceps.
And then make another incision near the first one, wait three days, and then scrape the sinew from the part above the vein until it is exposed.
And then elevate that and cut it in a second incision towards the ankle, and then pull it out and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out of the incision above it.
Do this until you pull it out of the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until all of it is out; and then cut it.
And if you are unable to pull it out and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and tie it, and pull it out, or insert a wedge underneath it and move your hand in all directions until it is pulled out. And be careful that it is not cut; for if it is broken, it will be difficult to elevate it much and it will cause harm to the patient.
And when you have completely removed it, then place on the affected area wool soaked in wine and oiled and unoiled oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having twists and turns to which it is wrapped, and is not straight as we said, then you must cut open the twist and the wrappings, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it completely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and harm it, for it will be difficult to lift it; and exercise your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not sharp like hooks, nor is it acutely pointed, so as not to injure the vein; and it is of considerable flexibility, lest, if it were subtle, it would cut the vein due to its thinness;  however, it has the appropriate thickness as we said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, where little vegetation grows.
And it is easily produced in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation comes from the putrefaction that lies beneath the skin, like snakes, worms, and insects that generate between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this [condition] is a violent inflammation in the leg where this vein appears; and soon the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the spot of that blister, appearing like the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap a piece of lead around it, weighing one drachm, or two drachms, and bind it, and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as any cause of it is found in the external parts, wrap it in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut off a portion of it and wrap the remainder, and do not cut into the root of it until it has come entirely out, because if you cut into it, it is constricted and enters the flesh, causing abscess and swelling at the location, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from behind it, and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, some men have had it extend to five palms and ten fingers, and it has come to my attention that one extended to twenty palms.
And if it is incised during its treatment, then insert a rod into its cavity and pierce it with a long instrument, until everything within the material is evacuated, and apply poultices to the area for several days using medicines; and then treat it as one would treat an abscess.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears near the joint of the foot or on the foot, and it has many openings, and a branch emerges from each opening.
And thus treat it as we have described in the division and with what has been previously stated.
Of the sectioning of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called bovine illness in some lands near us, because it often affects livestock, and is a small worm which is born between the skin and flesh, and travels throughout the body, up and down, and is felt by its movement from limb to limb until it bursts through the skin and escapes.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, maggots, and ascarids are produced in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it travels in the body and is raised to the head, and reaches the eye, then it strongly swells there and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is not done except by following its movement and appearance to the touch, and then it is necessary to tightly bind and constrict that which is above and below it, and then to cut above it and extract it.
And if it is hidden in the flesh and you cannot find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies, and the greatest cause to be feared is its corruption, which corrupts the area as we have said.
And if you see that creature having reached the head near the eye, then press on the brow with strong pressure, and then cut above it, and extract it; and it is necessary for the patient to use methods to cleanse their body of bad, putrid humors, and to avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
Of the incision upon the illness (or affliction).
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain which comes upon certain limbs, and then moves from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that, as I have told you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm. After remaining there for an hour, I saw that swelling move like a worm climbing, but more quickly than a worm could, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and settled in the muscle, and then I stayed there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other arm until it reached the other arm.
And then it returned to me, that it moved throughout her body as I saw; and I was greatly amazed by its movement and the speed from limb to limb. And I had already seen this before with my own eyes in that way, however, I have seen many cases where pain moves from limb to limb. But I did not see this with my own eyes in this way as in the woman.
And I did not consider it to be anything other than because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in this state the moving wind appeared to the senses.
And it is necessary that this type of remedy not appear in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to you with your eyes as we have said.  Then, lightly and gently press on it until that confined wind is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with body purging and with remedies that expel winds, and alleviate them with foul-smelling pills and with pills of herbs and similar ones.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types, and according to the parts of the body they pierce, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, that is, solid; and some have three angles, and some have tongues; and some have feathers.
And the signs of the brain are when an arrow strikes it, pierces the skull, and exits the cranium, which is above the brain, for there is a sudden violent accident, and fainting, dizziness, redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of thought and vomiting of bile, and the speech is often severed and the voice is lost, and a white humidity resembling dust comes out of the wound, and the area around it resembles the liquefaction of flesh.
Therefore, if these signs appear to you, then you refrain from the treatment and extraction of the arrow, unless it is on the surface.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, when it is near the left breast, are that it feels as if something hard, and not empty, has been thrust into the place; and often the movement of the arrow is similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the patient feels cold in the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; therefore, know that death is certain without a doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the outflow of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck become inflamed, and the patient's color changes, and he exhales heavily, and desires the intake of cold air.
And if the arrow strikes the diaphragm, which is in the chest, the pain is then near the small ribs, and a great tremor occurs with violent pain and sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then quite often undigested food comes forth from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is lodged within, there is some fissure or rupture of the tissue, or the bowel is torn, and there is no hope for the cure without the removal of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine leaks out, and some cause of this proceeds to the outside parts, and severe pain affects the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, and throat, shoulders and arms, the vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar limbs, are preserved according to their major dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery, and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I relate this to you, what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may interpret it for your own treatment.
The reason for this is that in a man, an arrow struck the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose, and I extracted it from the other side, under the ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the socket of his eye, beneath the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already embedded, and only a small tip with which it adhered to its shaft was recovered.  It was a large, pointed arrow, like those used by the Turks, with a lion's head at the tip and no feathers.
And the Jew was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man, which had been lodged in his throat. It was an Arab arrow, the kind with feathers.  It had already sunk into his throat; and so I carefully worked around it, and then extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man, which had lodged in his abdomen, and I thought he would die because of it.  However, after 30 days had passed and no change had occurred in his condition, I cut around the arrow and worked to remove it.  He was healed, and no further harm came to him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had lodged in his back, and the wound had healed.  But after approximately seven years, the arrow emerged from the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow had lodged in her abdomen. The wound had healed within her, and there was no change in her condition. The woman experienced no harm in any of her natural bodily functions.
And I saw another man in whose face an arrow had lodged, and the wound had healed.  The arrow remained, causing not much pain, and many similar cases like this one.
And I extracted the arrow from the King's prince's man, which had lodged in the middle of his nose and had already slightly deviated to the right side.  The entire arrow was hidden.
And I was called to treat him shortly after the arrow's lodging, within three days.
And then I found the arrow wound very tight; and then I probed it with a delicate probe, and felt nothing; but he felt a prickling beneath his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was from the arrow's tip.
And I applied a poultice to the area, containing virtues of attraction and digestion, confident that the area would become inflamed so that the arrowhead would appear to me, so that it would dry up on that spot.
And no significant accident occurred at the site to suggest that the arrowhead had entered that location; and I continued with the poultice on him for many days, and no accident occurred; and meanwhile, the wound healed, and the patient remained free from the procedure's effects for many days until one day he felt the arrowhead inside his nose and reported this to me.
Then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it opened up, and with the probe I tested it, and found the slender arrowhead, which was still attached to the shaft.
And then I gradually exposed the wound through the application of that sharp medicine until the arrowhead's tip became visibly apparent to me, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, as the wound had become inflamed, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and it remained, and did not yield to this approach, then I did not stop refining and devising methods, until one day, I used the forceps, skillfully made (as their design will be shown at the end of this chapter), to remove it; and then I healed the wound.
Some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose was not repairable, but I restored it, and the wound healed, and the patient was completely healthy, and suffered no further harm from it.
And I recount to you the method of removing some arrows, so that you may consider this a rule and understanding of what I have not said, because the parts of this art and the definitions of it cannot be fully grasped by narration, nor does the book fully encompass them.
And skilled craftsmen compare the little with the much; and what is present with what is absent; and they discover new operations and instruments for removing foreign bodies in the art when such an accident occurs.
Therefore, I say that arrows are not expelled from the members in which they are lodged except in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered; or by pushing from the other side.
And that which is expelled from the place where it entered, or is an arrow lodged in fleshy tissue, is pulled out and expelled.
And if you do not follow this procedure at the time of extraction, then you should leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh has healed, and the extraction and expulsion of the arrow is facilitated.
And similarly, if it is lodged in bone and you do not follow the procedure, leave it for several days and repeat the extraction with pulling and movement each day, and it will be expelled.
And if you do not follow the procedure for several days, then you must make a small incision around the arrow in the bone from all sides with a fine drill until the arrow's lodging is enlarged, and then extract it.
However, if the arrow is concealed within the body's tissues, and is hidden from sight, then you should search for it with a probe, and if you sense it, then pull it out with suitable tools for its removal.
And if you cannot do so due to the tightness of the wound and the arrow's depth, and if there is no bone, nerve, or vein involved, then you should spread the wound until it is widened and the arrow can be extracted.
And if it is lodged in the ears, then you should carefully separate the flesh in which it is lodged from all sides, using all your skill; and work on it until, if you cannot loosen the flesh around the fractured and twisted ear, it yields.
And when you are removing the arrow from any location, then use twisting motions of your hand with forceps on all sides until you force it to yield; and with utmost care, ensure the arrow does not break and that its extraction is not difficult for you.
And if you cannot remove it immediately, then leave it for a few days until the surrounding tissues have calmed, and then attempt its removal again when it is less inflamed. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then apply the treatments described in our chapter on healing.
And be cautious in cutting veins, nerves, or tendons, and employ every possible means to remove the arrow; do this with ease, calmness, patience, and firmness, as I have described.
And thus pull the arrow in the opposite direction to that in which the wound was created by the arrowhead, because this is more helpful. But if that is not possible, then apply whatever remedies are possible according to these methods.
However, if the arrowheads are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of the arrow is already outside the wound, or if the arrowhead's tip is curved from the point of impact on the deeper tissue, then you should make an incision over that. The incision should be in proportion to the size the forceps can grasp; then pull it out, for it will become more easily extracted.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrowhead enters the bone, and expands it, then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is also there, then you push it out with it.
But if the shaft is lodged and you want to administer a push, then you insert a curved instrument so that the curve enters the cavity of the arrowhead and the shaft, and then push it out with it.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then you push it out with a strong instrument entering it, because the arrowhead is eased by that.
And if the arrowhead is poisoned, then you must excavate all the flesh in which the arrowhead is lodged, if possible, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate.
And if the arrowhead lodges in the foot, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it and be careful not to injure a vein or nerve, remove it, and then cauterize the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
Form of forceps with which the arrows are extracted:
Let their extremities be like the beak of a bird, shaped like a saw, so that they grasp the arrow firmly and do not let go.
And of those [forceps] there are many varieties, large, small, and medium, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and the constriction of the area.
So that the instrument is easily extracted from the arrow's cavity and is pushed out with it.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which there are thirty customary ones, are described in chapter sixteen.  These include two pulsating veins near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins in the two visible temples; two veins in the two eye sockets, called *aspicientz*; the vein situated in the middle of the forehead; the vein located at the tip of the nose; two veins at the collarbone; two veins on the upper lip; two veins on the lower lip; the veins called *algelic*; and two veins below the tongue. However, the veins to be bled in the arms are five.  One is the cephalic vein, which lies beside the wild side of the arm; the people call it "the head vein," and it is a middle vein, composed of a branch of the basilica vein and another branch of the cephalic vein.  The people call it "the vein of the entire body".  The basilica vein lies on the domestic side of the arm, and is called "citillar", and the common people call it "the belly vein". There is also the vein of the arm, located above the elbow; it is the one bled and the one that appears conspicuously on the surface. There is also the salvatella, which lies between the index finger and the little finger, and is in two branches.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins. One of them is located below the bend of the knee on the wild side of the leg; the second is called *sophena*, and its location is below the leg on the domestic side; the third is the sciatic vein, located below the leg on the wild side; and there are other veins in the leg similar to these.
The bleeding of veins near the ears is for headaches, migraines, dizziness, and ancient sores/ulcers of the head.
And the method of bleeding those veins, as I recommend to you, is to first gently press the patient's head, and rub the patient's head at the back of the head (occipital region) in the place of both veins with a wide cloth using good friction; then gently press the patient's neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations, which are near the ears in two slightly indented spots on the head, appear; feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then proceed with the lancet.
Then take the lancet, which is the sharp one, and insert it below the vein along the groove until the bleeding reaches the bone. Then lift your hand along the vein and groove to the upper parts and cut the vein with a sharp incision. And make the incision the length of about two fingers, or thereabouts; and let the blood flow out as much as you desire.
Then apply compresses and leave them until it is healed.
And already in the beginning of the book was described the incision of these things and their cauterization.
But for the two arterial veins that are in the temples, the relief from phlebotomy is for chronic headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and the flow of acute excess humours to the eyes.
And the method of phlebotomizing those veins is as I recommend to you: hold the patient's neck until the two veins are clearly visible and the pulse of each can be felt below your finger; then you should tie them off with a ligature.
And then you raise the skin over the vein with your index finger, and insert the lancet underneath, and elevate the vein upward; and cut it just as you did the other two veins; and allow the blood to flow according to your need.
And then release the patient's neck restraint, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then apply a compress and powder to that spot and tightly bind it, and leave it until it is healed.
And the remembrance of their (the veins') location, and the incision and drawing (of blood), has already been described at the beginning of the book.

And bloodletting from this vein is safer than from any other vein, for below it there is no pulsating vein or nerve.
And bloodletting from the vein in the right wrist is beneficial for diseases of the liver.
And the procedure for bloodletting there is that you should hold the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after placing it in warm water, until the vein is swollen and clearly visible; then make a transverse incision on that vein; and if you cut it completely, that will do no harm.
And be careful not to extend your incision too far with the bloodletting, for beneath it are the nerves of the exposed fingers.
And then return the hand to the warm water and let the blood flow from it, for if you don't return it to the warm water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein, and the flow will be prohibited.
And when the blood has been drawn according to the necessary amount, then you place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not become firmly closed; and similarly, you should do this at all narrow passages.
However, bloodletting from the left hand is beneficial for illnesses of the spleen.
And do likewise in the bloodletting of him (or her) as you did in the other (hand).
And the benefit of bloodletting from the saphenous vein is for ailments that are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the mother (likely the uterus), and retention of menses, and for ailments of the kidneys and ulcers of the thighs and legs (chronic ones), and similar ailments.
And the procedure for bloodletting from him is that the patient should immerse their foot in warm water and rub it until the veins are clearly visible; and bandage the area around the foot with a strap; and the location of the vein that is clearly visible just above the toe, from there branches out into many small vessels along the surface of the foot.
And therefore, make the incision into the largest vein, either lower on the leg towards the heel, or closer to the joining point, for it is better and more beneficial.
And if you make the incision on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are beneath the surface of the foot, and make your incision across the vein, as if you were intending to cut it in half.
And let the bloodletting be done carefully.
But if the release of blood is prohibited, then you should repeatedly immerse the patient's foot in warm water and allow the blood to flow from it until it is evacuated.
And if the bloodletter makes a mistake on the first attempt, then repeat the procedure a little further, for the location is beneficial; and do not fear harming the patient, but rather, be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly, do the same for the vein of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, under the bone of the outer part.
And the benefit of the bloodletting for him is for pain in the hip when it is caused by hot blood.
And the procedure for his bloodletting is that the patient should enter a bath, and tightly wrap the thigh on the outside from above the heel for four days with a long, thin bandage, for it appears only through this.
And therefore, when it appears, then you should perform bloodletting of that vein, according to any technique possible and in the best way possible, or you cut it using incision or a cut, for its location is sensitive.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it does not appear at all by touch, then perform bloodletting on any branch of it, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot, above the so-called auricular and annular parts.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow out in the quantity you desire.
And then loosen the constriction and place a compress over the bloodletting site and tightly bandage the area, for it will heal quickly.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is of horns, or of earth, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and the removal of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping that is administered with incision and blood removal is applied to fourteen places on the body, of which one is at the back of the head; the middle place is on the two sides of the neck together;  and cupping on the chin below the jaw and below the mouth; and cupping on both shoulder blades; and cupping on the collarbone, and it is above the end of the breastbone; and cupping on the two elbows, and they are on the two arms; and cupping on the two forearms; and cupping on both thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from major veins, but rather from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not deplete the strength of those veins, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping with or without fire be done in any disease caused by fullness until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping for an illness or for a chronic condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end, and in the middle of the month, and at any time.
And therefore, when any of these conditions arise, especially if it is in the middle or third of the month, then we recommend cupping to be done after two or three hours have passed.
Cupping the nape of the neck is beneficial when it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is affecting the eyes; however, this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the location of a phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not recommended for someone with a cold or catarrh to use it, as it causes them significant harm.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for the elderly, or those with cold illnesses, to use it.
But for those who are continuously being treated in this manner, it is harmful;  therefore, you must instruct the cupper to slightly reposition their hand below the cup, in order to prevent the generation of harmful effects.
But cupping with alcohol is in the location of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica veins:  and thus it is beneficial for asthma, constriction of the windpipe,  rupture of the windpipe, coughing, and congestion.
And the alcohol cup should be slightly elevated, for if placed too low it will generate weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* is beneficial for accidental head pains, ophthalmia, migraines, fever, and toothache; and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And you must instruct the cupper not to press their hand with excessive force when scarifying, so as not to cut the artery and cause bleeding.
And cupping under the chin is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, and gum decay, and similar mouth ailments, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the veins in the lips.
But cupping performed above the two shoulder blades is beneficial for heatstroke, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two sides within the armpits is equivalent to the phlebotomy of three veins, namely the basilica, the cephalic, and the median cubital vein; because it draws blood from all those smaller veins within the flesh, and these small veins draw from the larger veins until the suction is carried out to the three main veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply with the scarification, because the area is lacking in flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping used on the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and sores beneath them.
And you must tell the cupper to use a large, well-made cupping glass, because the area requires a strong suction; and weak, flimsy cupping glasses are easily broken and should not be used for scarification.
But cupping on the legs is less effective due to its clear limitations, for it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for old pains in the kidneys, bladder, and uterus, and it can bring on menstruation, benefit from boils, and inflammations.  It is a substitute for bloodletting from two veins, and then a remedy for various ailments.
However, it often weakens the entire body and causes fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping in these cases is similar to the benefit of cupping on both legs.
And the correct application of cupping is to place the initially empty cupping glass and to apply a moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but place them gently, and keep them briefly, allowing the humors to move to the area by drawing them in. And do not stop repeating this process and continue until you see the area reddened and swollen, and the redness of the blood appears.
Then you should scarify and repeat the suction slowly, and then consider the condition of the body.
For a person with delicate, easily injured skin, you should scarify them only once, to avoid the area becoming ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to amplify the scarification slightly and to moderate the suction gently.
And if the blood is thick, then you should scarify twice, for in the first instance, the path for the blood and its watery part to be refined is opened. But in the second, the scrutiny gained from removing the thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then a third scarification should be performed to achieve the desired result.
And finally, when we want to extract a small amount of blood gradually, one scarification is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify many times; and if we know the blood is thick, then deep scarification is necessary.
And the measure of scarification is the depth of the incision only.
And if the area where the incision is to be made is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, the sites for the cupping glasses should be treated with ointments that are softening, opening, and resolving.  If it is summer, then this would include oils like almond, violet, mild camomile, or poppy seed oil. But if it is winter, then use narcissus, lily, chamomile, or bloodroot oils, or similar ones.
And if the excess is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth, balm, almond, or dill, and similar ones.
But if the area to be cupped has wide pores and tender flesh, then oil should be avoided.  Instead, the sites for the cupping glasses should be washed with cool water, water with leadwort, poppy water, or water with mullein, or similar preparations.
And if the person's blood is moist and watery, wash their cupping sites with sour wine, or water with myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for those with thick superfluities, wash the cupping sites with old wine, or water with yarrow (which is majorana), or a decoction of dill, or chamomile, or similar preparations.
And cupping should be avoided before a bath; if given, it should be administered shortly after the bath, within an hour or two, and the person should rest after cupping.
What is it that should be regulated in the case of cupping or bloodletting before the cupping?
You should consider at the beginning:  If the person being cupped or bled is choleric and has hot, inflamed blood, then they should take cooling things, such as milky salads, endive in sour wine, lettuce, seakale, juleps, and the like.
And give them foods like boiled greens, small birds' meats, and seasoned foods with acids, and similar things.
And for someone with a cold constitution, they should drink almond syrup, or syrup from sea-kale seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine (somewhat between old and new wine); and they should be told to consume little food and drink, and give them foods like greens, hemp seeds, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more plentiful than the food.
And it is necessary to give certain people, at certain times,  "tyriaca alpharac," or musk-containing remedies, or aloe vera before cupping or bloodletting, or soon after, so that the main members are strengthened and the blood is refined; and they should not take these warmed up.
But the cupping that is without incision should be cupping placed on the liver, spleen, gout, and on the navel, on the knee areas, and on the groin area, for these parts do not tolerate incision on them.
And that is not sought for except for drawing blood from the affected area, like placing a cupping vessel on the breast in a blood-flowing illness of the nasal passages; and we use it so that cold, sticky airiness is dissolved from the affected body part, like placing one on the belly and navel, because this refines the affected body part, warms it, and relieves the pain by dissolving the airiness.
And it is placed on the sinuses when there is blockage or phlegm in them, because through the power of strong suction it quickly clears the blockage and eradicates the phlegm from its location.
And similarly, it does the same when placed on the liver and spleen, to remove the airiness that is trapped there.
And these cupping vessels are administered empty, only with suction; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping vessel is full and larger; it is either just hot water or water in which herbs appropriate for this purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping vessel administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have shaped it; and the quantity of the hollow be half a palm; and it should be on its side, in the area of the curve, halfway, the hole should be small, such that a needle can just enter it.
Let it be made of red leather or citrus, with thick margins,  leather, or similar materials, so that it does not move the limb from its position over it.
And there should be a transverse rod of leather or iron in the middle, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping vessel should be larger than the one we have described; and the small one smaller than that, depending on the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping vessels for children and the weakened are different from those for young people and those with large bodies.
And the way to place this cupping vessel with fire on the limb is to heat the surface with fire from wood, properly using a small beeswax candle, and placing that in the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping vessel.  This is so the fire is kindled around the area below, ensuring the patient's body is not burned.
And then it is placed on the patient's limb, and the finger is placed on the opening we mentioned, until, when we have applied the cupping vessels and do not want the finger to be exposed and the vapor to escape through that opening, the cupping vessel is immediately closed.
And, afterwards, heat the surface according to its method, and repeat it if needed.
But a cupping vessel that is used in a pleural illness, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or opening, and is only applied filled with water, and placed directly on the limb.
And this is its form: and the larger this cupping vessel is, the better it is, because it can receive a larger quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not applied to many limbs, but to those where cupping is not possible due to their small size, such as the foot and the gums and similar parts; or to limbs lacking flesh, such as the finger and the nose and similar parts.
And the quality of using those leeches should be found in clear, fresh, spring water.
And they should be left in fresh water day and night until they are hungry, and no cause of fullness remains in their belly; and then the body should be emptied first with bloodletting and cupping; and then the patient's limb should be rubbed until it is reddened; and then the leeches should be placed upon it.
And when they are full and drop off.
And if suction from the area is possible with a cupping glass, it is better for the healing.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with clean water; and rub and squeeze it.
And if the body of blood extends near the biting place of the leeches, and it is oozing, then you immerse a cloth in cold water, and place that over it until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle powdered vitriol, or other similar astringent substances, on it, until the blood is stopped; or place roasted chickpea husks on the area, and leave them until the husks adhere to the area, and the blood is stopped.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when possible to change them.
And if the leeches are prohibited from being applied, then rub the area with fresh blood, or prick the area with a needle until blood flows; and then apply them, for they quickly adhere when they sense blood.
And when you want them to drop off, sprinkle a little powdered alum, or salt, or ash, on them, and they will fall off quickly.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish physicians and the vulgar, and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a letter from them, boast of this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of the learned here in our land is private; and I have not forgotten this at all; and I have not adopted this that I have learned, except through the length of reading the books of the ancients and study on understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
And I will now turn to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have now described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experience has passed; since I have shortened this for you and freed it from lengthy explanations, and condensed it at the end of the condensation; and shown it at the end of the declaration; and formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as is done by the addition of explanation, just as I have done in the two previous chapters.
First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the essential aspects of bone fractures, those prerequisites needed before we begin discussing the repair of fractured and dislocated limbs of all kinds. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the chapters, the understanding of which is necessary for the craftsman and the standing upon the truth of those; that is, you and those who are ready to learn or acquire this noble art alongside you.
And so I say that when you encounter a fracture, dislocation, twist, or injury, then you must initially apply bloodletting or leeching, or both, if no other reason prohibits it, such as a weakness in their constitution, or if the person injured is a child, or an elderly person in decline, or if it is a time of extreme heat or extreme cold.
And afterwards, let their diet consist of cool herbs and the flesh of fowl and rabbits; and avoid heavy meats and filling foods, and any food that causes the veins to swell with blood, until you are certain of a controlled, hot abscess, and unless you fear the efflux of matter to the limb.
And then restore the patient to their original regimen, which is the one they were accustomed to.
And so, once the broken bone begins to be restored, the patient must eat nutritious foods that are nourishing, thick, and substantial, such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, rice, bread, peas, cow's stomachs, eggs, fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things.  With this regimen, the fracture's coagulation will happen more easily and better.
And know that broken bones in people who are in their prime and in the elderly, are not likely to be completely joined and healed naturally because of the wasting of their limbs.
However, nature joins and connects to the broken bone from all sides a certain glue-like substance that is thickened, which adheres and firmly holds it until it is completely joined and bound together, until it reaches a state of strength and firmness, just as a fire at the beginning until nothing obstructs its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient eat foods that are heavy, viscous, and substantial, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly all other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is diverse types of fractures within themselves.  (Or:  A variety of fractures exist within each type.) And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is a bow-shaped fracture, and others that occur in star-like patterns; and sometimes a fracture occurs along the bone's length: and these are fractures of the bone's surface and edges and additional [fractures] proceeding and not proceeding [from the main fracture], just as in a quill pen when it is prepared for writing. And there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for each type [of fracture], there is a specific treatment or remedy in restoring it, according to the separate mention of it in its place.
A variety/different sign of a bone fracture. And by this is a bone fracture known: it is its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and this is when you press upon it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting at the affected spot, and it is not painful to the touch when you press on the bone with agitation, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture.
However, it is possible that there is a twisting or a small fissure, so you should not examine it with your hand and compression, but instead, place on it the medicinal remedies for which you remember the one suitable for the time; and then be strict with a subtle constriction.
And understand that when the site is bent but not cleft in two or otherwise dislocated, as if a whole part of its alignment is off, then you must begin immediately to rectify it, lest a suppurating boil occur there.
And if a suppurating boil does occur there, then leave it for some days until the boil subsides; and then bandage it according to every method you can of lightness and ingenuity.
And understand that the restoration and straightening of a displaced part is easier than that of a fractured bone.
And then strengthen it according to the remembrance of how to do it.
But if the bone is dislocated, then extension of the fractured limb (whether hand or foot) is necessary; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with one rope and your hand.
And maintain the position in which you place the limb in a bandage according to its natural shape, while the parts of the fractured limb are extended, then exert yourself to return those additions with ingenuity and according to every method you can to their places.
And be careful, according to your strength, that you do not cause pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and try to the best of your ability to apply one bone to its companion, according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that time you carefully touch it with your hand, and if you find any other cause, correct it and make it whole with the strength you possess.
Avoid excessive extension and compression, as some foolish physicians often cause abscesses or contractions in the limb through their operations, as we have seen in their many operations.
And the proper degree of immobilization of the calmed limb is what I recommend; so you should know that broken limbs vary in their size, and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and the like, the wrapping should be of fine, soft cloth.
But those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, require a firm, strong wrapping, for a firm bandage connects the large limb and binds it tightly everywhere, so that nothing penetrates it.
And then, carefully, apply a suitable bandage to the site of the fracture, using three or four layers of bandage, depending on the limb's needs.  Press your hand slightly against the bandage.
And now move towards the most superior part of the fracture site, and tighten the bandage less than you did initially when you tightened the fracture site. And then gently extend the bandage around the fracture site a little, gradually loosening the tightness until you have moved a considerable portion of the limb.
And now take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site, carefully.  Then move the bandages towards the lowest part of the fracture; and let your action involve the tightness of the wrapping and the gradual loosening of it, according to what we have described in the first wrapping above.
And then place between the bandage layers a linen or cloth pad to cover any twisting of the fracture, if there is any twisting; otherwise, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over these carefully made layers immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area.  If there is, apply something to reduce the abscess and inflammation. Leave it for a few days and then tighten the layers.
Let the splints be made of half of a sturdy, properly shaped branch, or from pinewood, or palm ribs; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others, and its length should match the limb's size; then, wrap the splints with another bandage, tightening as in your first wrapping; then tie it firmly on top, as we described about tightness, and make sure that your tightening is more directly over the fracture site, and the further away from the fracture, the looser the wrapping.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fine, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some fools do in their operations, using ropes or twisted cordage, that is a major mistake, as it disrupts the even tightening.
Similarly, excessively fine threads are not suitable, for you will not be able to achieve the desired tightening with them.  And there should be at least one finger's width between each splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the ends of the splints near the tightening in sensitive areas, then place some soft linen or carded wool underneath them to prevent injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption of the bone occurs. I will address this in the second chapter, discussing its uniqueness.
And know that not every broken limb should be splinted on the first day; this is because, when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it until approximately five, seven, or more days, depending on your assessment of the risk of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients restored fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of a common plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it does not cause heat or cold:  take pulverized flour, specifically the medulla of the flour that settles on the walls of the mill after grinding.
And mix it as it is, but sift it with egg white, and make a mixture that is neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: a recipe of asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild carrot root (migrach), of each a handful; of myrrh and aloes, a handful each; of aloes, two handfuls;  all ground and sifted, mixed with warm water or egg white if the patient's complexion is hot. Then use this plaster, as it is a good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of plaster, beneficial for fractures and sprains: a recipe of wild carrot root (migrach) and white marshmallow root (met de altea blanca), a handful each; of myrrh and aloes, half a handful each; of acacia, a little more than half a handful; of Armenian bole, two handfuls.
All are ground in a mortar with great care, and sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and for pain, fissures, and fractures: take a piece of linen cloth, soak it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but is useful for a hot abscess and expels pain effectively.
Another method of plaster to restore a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind them together and make a plaster from them.
A quicker method of plaster, useful for the restoration of a fractured bone and when you want resolution of remaining abscesses: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, violet flowers, and the flour of yarrow, and mix them thoroughly. Grind them all together and mix them with wine, if the affected limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then you mix it with damp coriander water or with water and use it.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving a hard abscess, to aid in the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow, seeds of lily, and balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, and mix them together thoroughly. All must be ground up and mixed with willow water, or plain water, or wine, depending on the limb's condition and color.
However, Hippocrates in his book did not say that a plaster should be placed on a broken limb for its restoration, but rather a cerot (plaster) made of wax and oil, and nothing else; and he recommended that it be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is observed that such plasters are placed on a broken limb to aid in its restoration, and the ingredients in which there is drying with a small amount of warmth, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar substances to those we have mentioned.
A plaster for injuries and pain: take equal parts of figs, human skin (or bird skin), marshmallow, and salt; grind them, sieve them, and mix them to form a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain in place, and when it should be removed, depends on whether there is no pain, inflammation, or the bone is not displaced from its position, then it should not be loosened for a few days. And if there is severe pain, inflammation, or swelling at the location, then you should remove or loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster from it.
And take a linen cloth and a dampened sponge of the sea-lion, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the affected area until the inflammation ceases and the pain subsides; then allow the limb to rest for an hour; and then place a layer of wool dipped in sour wine and oil or rubbed oil upon it, and bandage it for a night, until you are certain of any abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb is reduced and the pain from it is removed.
And then repeat that fine compression and small plaster, and do not tighten it with your initial constriction, and continue with it until it is healed.
And if the abscess, stiffness, pain, inflammation, and all the other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need the plaster and compression, then you repeat it just as you did at the beginning.
And if no cause of those we have mentioned afflicts the limb, then do not loosen it for only three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes all of this is left for twenty days, depending on how the limb responds to the loosening, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh coagulates around it.
Then increase the compression more than in your initial compression, and increase the patient's nourishment, in accordance with the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fracture site is already drying out and weakening more than it should, then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, moisten it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it.  This should be done every three days, and loosen the compression slightly.  Through this procedure, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers of limb fractures do another time, when it hasn't been properly restored at first, and is restored in a crooked way, is an error according to their procedure and a great danger.  If this were right, the ancients in their books would have magnified it and would have acted accordingly.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, which makes it more reasonable that one should not do it.
Second Chapter: Of fractures when occurring to the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a blunt object, and the blow crushes all the bone inwards to the skull, as happens to a wooden post.  This type is therefore called a comminuted fracture; or the blunt object cuts a part of the bone and cuts into the flesh but does not pass through to the back of the bone; and this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the severity of these two types of fractures can be significant or minor;  and a fracture that is a crushing or grinding type is caused by a blow with a rock or a fall on a stone, and similar impacts.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone.  The severity of these two types of fractures can be significant or minor.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the subtlety of a small hair, and therefore this type is called a hairline fracture.
And a fracture is caused either by a fall or a blow from a rock, or similar impacts, that forces a piece of the bone inward. This happens in a concave location, just as the ribs are affected when struck and they are fractured inward.
This is more common in the bones of the young, as is often seen in the heads of children.
And in all these types of fractures, the bone fragments are scattered, like separated stars. And I will proceed with the repositioning of everything to its proper place.
And we diagnose all these types of fractures by examination of them, and by investigation using probes and examining the decaying flesh on top of them.
A specific type of fracture is recognized by the fact that it is exposed on the bone, and it is cleaned and examined with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms, and if you observe symptoms that indicate a serious condition, such as vomiting, restlessness, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, swelling and stiffness of the limbs, and similar severe symptoms, without a doubt, it is not going to be successfully treated.
And if you observe symptoms that are not severe, you hope for recovery, then you begin the treatment of the patient.
The reason for this is that if the afflicted person comes to you at the beginning of the affliction, and this is during the winter days, then you should study the condition of the bone before the 14th day according to all aspects of the situation.
And if it is during the summer days, then you should attempt to set the bone before the 7th day, before the area beneath the bone becomes corrupted by inflammation, and the symptoms we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the inflammation covering the brain, and it is with broken and rubbed pieces, then you must cut out the broken and rubbed pieces, as I will describe to you.
And you should carefully shave the head of the afflicted patient, and expose the bone according to the customary and suitable method, as closely as possible to the nature of the affliction, and with gentleness toward the patient.
And if you find a bone exposure, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary.  Fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rubbed oil, and allow the affliction to rest until the abscess subsides and the blood flow stops; then begin with the encirclement of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a straight-edged scalpel, and this is its form:  And then administer another scalpel, slightly wider than the first; and this is its form:
And then administer a scalpel wider still than the second.
And it is necessary for you to have several different scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with sharpened edges on their ends, and made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking on the scalpel, so that you do not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to bore around it before you use the scalpels with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not named thus except because they do not reach the surface of the bone near it, for the drill has a rounded end or edge, and one that is below the sharp head, like a small edge or circle, that prevents it from sinking into and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each appropriate to the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand for each skull a drill whose end is sharp, and whose length and shortness corresponds to the thickness of the skull.
And this is the way for three types of drills:  To drill around a fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone is penetrated; then change the drill to another location and place the appropriate amount between each hole, equivalent to the thickness of the ray or approximately that.
Then cut with scalpels what is between each hole in the bone, making it as thin as possible, as we said, until you have removed the bone, either with your hand, or with some other tool, such as pliers and delicate forceps.
And you must take great care that the scalpel does not touch anything of the spinal cord.
And therefore, once you have drilled around the bone, and if it is connected to the spinal cord, you must separate it, and extract it.  You must scrape and smooth the rough edges of where the bone was connected to the spinal cord using a similar tool to a scalpel, but one that is more pointed and delicate.
And if any small pieces of bone remain and are loose, then carefully remove them with the tools available to you; then treat the wound with liniments and ointments, as we have described.
And another method of operation is gentle, free from fear, which Gualdo praised greatly; and this is his teaching:  It is necessary for you, at the beginning, to begin by uncovering the area of the bone where the fracture is most severe and apparent, until you have uncovered that area and placed the tip of this gentle and pleasant scalpel, whose form is this:
And the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part has two sides extending lengthwise, just like a blade; so that the lenticular part lies against the spinal cord with the sharp side of the blade cutting into the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on a part with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone with ease as it is loosened, and you are certain that no accident occurs, so as to avoid any feared event.  And if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of their craft; truly, even if they were sleeping.
Then if any piece remains attached to the bone in any part of the bone, cut it off with the lenticular end of the scalpel, and remove it easily, for it is detached without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the tissue, and whatever is cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughnesses and subtle irregularities, then it is necessary to scrape off those roughnesses and eradicate those irregularities with delicate razors, for which you have prepared a variety of sizes so that you can apply the most appropriate ones in every location and the most direct ones according to where you are in your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your procedure, with which you scrape the bone initially, you use the widest of those razors, and then use the more delicate ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you have reached the finest and most delicate of all of them.
However, for each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must apply treatment according to what you see as needed for its repair.
And this is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And what we have written about this is demonstrated in great fractures.
And if the skin is retracted when you expose the bone, then you must take a cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in warmed oil, and place it over the wound opening; and then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in warmed wine and oil, and place that over the first cloth, doing this as gently as possible so you do not cause further injury; and then apply a wide bandage, and do not tie it too tightly, but only enough to hold the wound together.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until you are sure there is no further inflammation; then, remove it, and apply medicines to counteract dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, ashes, and similar powders.
And you should make powder from these medicines, ground into a powder, and apply it to the wound, as they are dry; and in general, use any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to sting.
