And be careful, according to your skill, that the wound remains clean during your treatment; and do not allow any suppuration or other foulness to accumulate there; for when such things gather on the skin covering the brain, they corrupt it and cause harm, and this will lead to severe illness.
And harm comes to the skull, especially when the bone is exposed, and particularly when the treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on the surface of the wound; and if you see these harmful symptoms in the patient, know that it is dangerous without a doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the wound itself, but from the medicine applied, and if the medicine is causing this blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of olive oil, and with this, moisten a cloth, and place it on the skull; and then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two parts simultaneously, but sometimes in one part, because there are two bones in the nose; for the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken, and there is only friction, twisting and bending to it.
However, if one of the two parts of the nose is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose and use your index finger to push that fracture inward, and your thumb outward, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause the patient pain through your actions.
And if the fracture is in the most prominent part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be set with the tip of a tool that is slightly thickened.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should do the same.
And strive for the patient's restoration on the first day after the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately seven or ten days later, apply a soothing poultice.
And approximately insert a piece of linen cloth into the nostril, if the fracture is in one part, or two pieces, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the thickness of the cloth fill the nasal opening.
Some ancient healers advised that the cloths be soaked in broth and changed daily, but I do not find this appropriate.  Instead, the cloth should be soaked in a mixture of beaten egg white and powdered spice; and leave it in place until the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a cloth, a bird's feather, wrapped in linen, is inserted into the nose;  this is done when the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not forbidden from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; if you wish, do this, or if you wish, use cloths.
And if, on another occasion, a purulent abscess forms, plaster the nose with a cerot or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil, or with diachylon.
But if no purulent abscess forms, then you should cover the outside with sesame flour and powdered cumin mixed with egg white;  then place a linen bandage over that, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the bones of the nose are fractured or broken into small pieces, then you should cut around them, and remove those pieces with instruments appropriate for that; then close the fracture and heal it with things that promote fleshing and consolidation, and with suitable plasters.
And if an ulcer occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with cloths and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an ulcer, then observe if the fracture is only superficial, and not broken in two but bent inward, then its reduction is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of your right hand; and push with that the prominence of the fracture inwards with ease towards the outside, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; with that gently align it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension of both parts according to the straight line until its possible alignment.
And if you encounter a loosening or displacement of teeth, then bind those that you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; and then place a compress on the broken jaw, and then place over that a folded cloth, and then place over the cloth a large, properly made poultice, or a piece of lead of the same length as the jaw; and then bandage that accordingly, as your method of bandaging is prepared and the application is appropriate, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and to consume nourishing, light foods.
And if you estimate that the shape has already been altered in any way, then you should carefully remove and loosen it on the third day; and then correct what has been altered on it and plaster it with powdered chalk, with egg white, or with flour of figs, after you have removed the compress from it, and place a soft wool bandage over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and fitted to it, and the bone's position is not altered, leave it; do not remove it until it is healed and the fracture is set; for many times this fracture is set in three weeks.
And if, another time, a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said about its treatment until it is healed and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or splinters; then you should carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for their removal from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should enlarge it with leeches according to your needs.
And after you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the appropriate plasters on it and encourage the healing, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when fractured.
Often the collarbone is broken on the part towards the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts, apart from any splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it happens with splinters, and is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with an open wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without an open wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the injured and fractured collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
And then manipulate the fracture with your fingers until it is in proper shape, and is neither raised excessively nor concave.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, and its size according to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have aligned the fracture correctly.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's extremity outward, because it has become jammed, then the patient must be placed supine on their back, and place a cushion of medium size on the muscle above their neck.
And the assistant presses the muscle below until the collarbone's bone is lifted out of the jam, and then you straighten the fracture and manipulate it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters or sharp pieces of the collarbone, then it is necessary to cut them and remove those splinters easily.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, having placed under the collarbone the instrument that serves as a lever; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of this shape:
And it is similar to a scoop, of which this is the concave part; and let its width be according to your needs, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as much as possible for the operation; it should have two ends like a wedge, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the gap of the wound you have made when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it is a feverish abscess, then you should bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the gap is small, or you fear a feverish abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if a feverish abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rubbed olive oil and wine, and place it over it.
And take care that the bandage does not move from over the broken bone.  Therefore, you should see the patient each day, and when you see that the ligament is softened or the bandage has moved, then you should adjust it and tighten the binding.
Then place the patient's sleep, the sleep of the patient, on the patient's neck, and place a small cushion under the patient's armpit in the sleep of the patient during the night; so that with that the patient's injury is raised from the side of it, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is raised to the level of the muscle; or the patient's arm is bound to the patient's neck.
And do not loosen the bandage if there is not swelling or abscess in the area, for up to twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the patient's skin reacting to it, and adjust the binding and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and coagulated.
And it is tightened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fractured shoulder.
The shoulder blade is fractured at a point lateral to the patient and not fractured through, only the edges.
And therefore, when the fracture is located there—and is fractured in the middle of it—and is only known by touch, then, according to the shape of the fracture, you must prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible.
Then place on the area powdered myrrh with white of egg and stop the bleeding; then place a folded cloth pad; then place a thin, wooden splint over it, matching the size of the entire shoulder blade, or a little more.
But if below the splint there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with styptic, and then adjust the splint to make it even.
And then tie it tightly above with a long bandage, with appropriate constriction and firmness to the splint, until it is no longer displaced from its place.
And check the bandage each day; and every time it is softened, bandage it again and tighten it, and reposition the splint if it is displaced from its place.
And let the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade will be restored in twenty or twenty-five days.
And therefore when this number of days is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and you should know for certain that the union of the bones is not threatened nor is their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the heavy bone and place the splint beneath it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we said regarding the fracture of the clavicle's healing from abscesses if an abscess arises here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes breaks in the middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much more slowly, being crushed and splintered.
And among the complications when the middle of the foot is broken, there is a sinking of the parts inward, causing great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, severe fever, and vomiting blood; and the broken bone's cavity is apparent and not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it involves placing the patient on their back, and placing a cushion between their shoulders; then press on their muscles and adjust their sides with the hands of two assistants, and be subtle in the procedure and in aligning everything according to all possible means and necessities, until the bone's shape returns to its proper form.
And then apply a poultice and bandages, and place a thin splint of willow or reed, or similar lightweight material, then wrap it in cloth; and then be subtle in binding that around the broken bone so that it does not move; and proceed with wrapping in a circular manner around the back sometimes, and tighten it decently; and then continually monitor the bandage, and when softened, tighten it further.
And if necessity arises due to loosening, corrosion, swelling, pain, or abscess that develops in the area, then you loosen and remove it, remove the poultice, and correct what has happened through the method of treatment; and then reapply the poultice, if needed, to the area; and maintain the bandage until healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of the ribs when fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs occurs only in the thick parts, which are on the back; and in the extremities and front portions, they do not suffer damage of this kind, because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense, through examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is aligned with the fingers in the best possible way, until it is aligned as necessary, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if it is needed.
And if the rib fracture is leaning towards the internal parts, then the patient experiences severe pain and stabbing pain, just like the stabbing pain that afflicts those with pleurisy, and because of this, the bone pierces the lining; and there is difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is a difficult condition to cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in dealing with that with many inventions.
And from their teachings, it is said: you must restrict the patient's food that engenders inflation and windiness, so that the belly is inflated and stretched, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
However, we would avoid this, because it is not a light cause to generate a hot abscess if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, then help is needed for that.
And some of them would say that a cupping glass should be placed over it and that it should be sucked with force; and it seems like a rational approach, however, it is feared that the cupping glass draws the superfluities to the location because of the weakness.
And some of them would say: the area must be covered with wool that has been soaked in warm oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the dressing is tight when it envelops that according to its curvature; and then the patient should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause afflicts the patient, whom it cannot withstand, and the bone pierces the membrane with a painful puncture, causing us concern about the patient, then we must open up the area, expose the broken rib, and then place an instrument on top that keeps the structure in place, the method of which is established beforehand, and cut the bone with ease and extract it.
And then we must stitch the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if there is an abscess inside, then you take and mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and let the patient be treated with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Sometimes there are fractures in the bones of the neck, and sometimes there is damage to their articulation, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you find their muscles already softened, numb, dead, and you cannot move them, stretch them or squeeze them, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in such a state of disrepair it is fatal.
And if you move them and feel a constriction with your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's integrity is intact and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar signs in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then, if you see that the feet are also softened, like the muscles, and when they lie on their back, they experience involuntary movements and spasms, and sometimes they lie on their back wanting to urinate but cannot, then know that this is fatal, and do not try to treat them.
But if you don't observe any of those symptoms, the cause is better and less severe.
And the treatment for this is to apply a soothing poultice made from heated oil or crushed egg yolks to the affected vertebra.  Apply this for several days, until the inflamed area is soothed.
Then apply a comforting and stimulating plaster, and bandage it securely.  Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe signs of bone fracture or any other cause that separates the bone from its surrounding tissue, then you must cut away the surrounding flesh and remove the bone.  Then rejoin the two edges of the wound with stitches, if the gap is large, and treat the area with a healing ointment until it heals.
But if the back of the bone is fractured, and it is the end of the process, then you must insert the tip of your left hand into the rectum, and hold the fractured bone in place with your other hand as best you can to align it properly. Then apply a plaster and bandage, if necessary, and secure them firmly.
And if you find fractured bones there, then remove them and treat the wound with the previous methods until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] jaw.
The bones of the jaw are often fractured, and when they are, the fracture is usually only a separation of the ends, and they are jagged along their length, and they shift away from each other.  The patient experiences pain and tenderness in the area, and the limb is numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it so that it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at one end, then you align that end so that it is matched to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along the length or has already shifted, then you have the patient lie on their stomach until you are ready to restore that fracture. And so, when you have aligned it, place a poultice over it, and then place a splint of wood or something similar over it, and tightly secure it so that you don't worry about any further fracture or movement of the splint.
And align the cavity with whatever fills it until it reaches a tight fit, and tell the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a painful abscess develops, then you stop the extension and restoration until the painful abscess is calmed, and apply what calms it, as was previously stated; then return to the restoration, and secure it properly.
And if the bones are shattered or splintered, then you must set them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of a fractured bone.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder's head.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness according to this method:
And two ligaments are tied to the two extremities, and then it is suspended from a raised position.
And place the patient on a bed; then gently lay the patient's broken arm on the wood until the joint of the arm (the point where the arm bends) is aligned with the middle point where the curve of the wood is.
And then suspend some heavy object over it, or the attendant extends the wood below; and then the physician holds the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
The other method is that the patient is placed on their back and the hand at the neck is supported by a bandage; and then command two attendants, one to hold what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other to hold what is below it; and each of them should extend it to their side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tighten the bandage over the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two attendants should extend it to their sides.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed over the joint and the other below the fracture, towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that area and over the elbow.
Apply that with ease and without trouble, and then, when it is properly aligned and applied with good application, tighten it if no suppurating boil occurs.
But if a suppurating boil occurs, then leave the tightening until the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the boil subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a bandage of clean cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm on that support, and place their open hand on their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in the proper position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And you place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the others; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the break, with an additional three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the constriction, which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your constriction over the fracture site is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the constriction should be.
And thus, adjust the placement of the splints and the constriction as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position; then you do [the procedure].
And if you fear a suppurative abscess, then leave the constriction and splints on for seven days, as we have said.
Then consider the ligaments in all parts for three days, so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the area; or food should be withheld from the limb due to the excess of constriction; and then correct everything according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the binding until approximately three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place a poultice of full wool on the aid, and be careful at all times of night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted, or that the ligament is not softened; then correct this all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we previously mentioned, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until the swelling has begun to subside; and then it is necessary to increase the amount of food.
Because it is customary for the poultice and the limb to be swollen for 40 days; and then it is necessary to loosen them and administer baths and medicate with ointments that correct it.
And if the fracture is badly afflicted, then you do not loosen the ligament and splints until after several days or even two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called two "foci"; of which one is small, and it is that which supports the said finger, and the other is large and is the one that lies beneath the small finger, on the lower side.
And frequently only the larger "focus" is broken, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the small "focus" is fractured above, then the restoration of it is easier, and its healing is more straightforward.
And when the "focus" of it is fractured below, the fracture's repair is difficult, and its healing is challenging.
And even more difficult than that is when both bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the fractured bone is the small "focus" above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during the restoration, to apply a small amount of extension with ease until it is united.
But if the large "focus" is fractured, then it requires a more forceful extension.
And if both "foci" are fractured together, then you must apply the constriction much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the shape of a hand during the restoration and the extension, laid out on a cushion, with the wrist of the hand higher than all the other fingers, and the thumb lower than the other fingers, and the patient be seated on a smooth, hard surface.
And place a cushion in front of him, at his height or level, so that the patient does not experience strain.
And then one attendant extends the lower arm with their hand or bandage, and another attendant extends the upper arm similarly.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it returns to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are irregularities, then you must use all your skill to reposition each piece in its proper place.
And if there appears a separate irregularity that punctures the skin and you are not hopeful of restoring it, then you must operate on it and remove it according to the method previously described.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter about this, and therefore follow the treatment of that.
And if you encounter a boil at the start of your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of rosemary oil and white wax, and it should be between specifically delicate and subtle, and tightly bandage the cloth over that until the boil subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a preparation of ground powder mixed with egg white, and then place plasters; and let the plaster placed over the fracture be the most suitable and strongest.
And know that the types of plasters for the arm are found in the most important arrangements, meaning fractures in one or two compartments at once.
And then apply your bandage to the fracture site more strongly and vigorously, and if the bandage is placed on the upper parts of the fracture or on its lower parts, apply the bandage a little more gently, according to what was previously described in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, and not hard or rough, and the bandage should be of a medium tightness, neither too loose nor too tight, just as we have previously described.
And observe the limb and its ligament for a few days. And if any cause requiring correction arises, such as swelling on the limb, then you should immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the constriction has softened and the bone has rejoined and looks normal, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction, for then you must soften that slightly and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then bind it firmly again.
But if the patient does not exhibit any of the conditions we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be removed for approximately 20 days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's arm should be suspended by its own power of active movement, and have them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or a very mild one is restored in 28 days, and all of this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the disposition of the healing power.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the hand's wrist and fingers.
Know that the wrist of the hand and the joints of the fingers are often fractured, and they are injured not only by impact but also by repeated rubbing.
And when the hand is injured by fracture or rubbing, then the patient must be laid down flat and a bandage placed on it evenly, and then the patient's hand should be placed on that bandaged area.
And then the attendant extends the broken bones and the physician binds them together until they are properly joined.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, lest there be a suppuration; and then place a splint over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make something like a ball of cloth, and order the patient to hold it with their fractured palm; then it should be bandaged with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather covered with cloth, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and bandage it as necessary.
However, if the fracture is in the outer parts, then you must place a splint on top and another splint underneath on the palm of the hand, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, just as the hand is wrapped around the thumb with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs to any of the bones of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be splinted as necessary, and then bandaged with the palm; and if desired, place a small, straight splint on it, so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or pinky finger, then it should be splinted and bound with the adjacent finger; or all fingers should be bound together, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and then that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose it—with your operation—with what is necessary when any such cause occurs, according to how it is iterated in the narration.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken, and it is recognized by its movement being shifted forward and to its most prominent part.
And the restoration of it is done so that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament under the fracture, and the patient is laid prone on their stomach and on their face.
And then the attendant stretches all the ligament to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be close to the knee, in the direction of the hip socket, so that the stretching pushes against it, and the other ligament under the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is close to the knee, then the ligament should be placed close to the knee, and the stretching should be done in the parts below.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a poultice and a bandage, lest the affected limb becomes infected. But if it becomes infected, then you must leave it for several days until the acute inflammation subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, in the lower parts, and let the ends of the splint extend from the most superior part of both areas. Then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and the thigh.
And then place splints on the thigh over the fractured area, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the gaps between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is secured.
And then begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture area, using three or four wraps.  As you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, it should become progressively lighter, softer, and looser.
Then extend the two ends of the splint to the lower parts until they reach the ankle of the foot, and firmly hold both ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not shift from its position.
And then leave the bandage on it, ensuring that no corrosion, boil, inflammation, or similar problems occur on the limb; and if any of these conditions arise, then remove it gently, and correct whatever has happened immediately, as we have often said.
However, if there is a bone fragment lodged in the bone, then you should remove it as best as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it, and treat it with what preceded the reminder until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh or other parts are splinted, just as we have said for injuries to the arm.
However, in a restoration done in this manner, the patient does not experience limping; and if it is restored alone, or if the other leg is applied, then it is necessary that the patient limp with the affected leg.
And know that the thigh is splinted for 40 days, or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fractured kneecap/patella.
Know that a fractured kneecap/patella often heals, but it sometimes results in a lot of friction/abrasion.
But if it results in a fracture, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a plaque (a fragment), and on this, everything has been determined through sensation (examination).
And the restoration of it is thus, that which is broken in its parts is joined together by means/methods until it is joined and connected as much as possible by equalizing, facilitating, and working/manipulating.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round patch, if you need that one; and bandage tightly with a bandage suitable for this.
And then consider all the methods we have described in other fractures, like an abscess and similar situations, in such a way that it opposes any accident with what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is thin, and is called the shin bone.
And the treatment for any of the types of fractures that affect the two bones of the arm, is done similarly to the restoration of the arm, and the procedure is the same.
And if both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is dislocated in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is dislocated forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it underneath, and it is for those reasons that are not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and bandaging of the splints, accordingly.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should alleviate it with your skill.
And if the leg requires some operation on the arm, and when you adjust the splints and complete all your operation, then you shall take pieces of light wood, which are placed below the supports that are placed on cushions, and are easily made of palm branches; choose those with a small thickness, and do not use any thick ones, and their length should correspond to the length of the leg, from the top down to the lowest part.
And then wrap it with cloth in two layers along its length, and place one layer over the affected part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and let them extend from the top down to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places, at both ends and in the middle, for with this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and is in a good position.
And now a small wooden support has been placed, corresponding to the length of the leg; and the leg is placed on it to prevent any movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this in cases of fracture, and when it is properly a broken bone.
And from now on, examine the leg each day and study it further, and you should watch for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar conditions; and if any of these occur, then apply whatever is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel bone and bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel does not occur often; but in the bones of the foot, fractures occur sometimes; and in the toes of the foot, fractures rarely occur, and they are affected only by friction during the process of the body’s positioning.
And if the bones of the foot suffer a fracture and the bones are sometimes raised above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared as if walking.
And then place one foot on what is elevated by those bones, and press down on those bones, and squeeze, and push them until they return to their place.
And then place a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small table under the sole of the foot with two flat ends.
And then secure that with a downward pressure of the foot, since you have wrapped it with bandages and other things as needed.
And after three or four days, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones either reduced (put back in place), or broken or dislocated, and the procedure for these is as you know.
But if any fingers are broken, then restore them, and set them in place as I have and recounted to you in the restoration of fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and let it be slightly wider than the finger; and then place the previously described table under the foot, and secure it with downward pressure.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its own size wrapped in linen, and secure the table on the bottom of the foot, and let it be placed on one end of the table over the front of the foot, so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is fitting for you not to forget that you must consider all the things that have been said to you in the other fractures and accidents which we have discussed; and be prepared for every accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis of the male.
When the thigh bone is broken, then make that incision in a curved fashion, and then slightly incline it towards the back, and let it be held behind.
Then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be done to the thigh in this way; then the woman should be calmed, and raise her back little by little, for all of that is done until the broken bone is back in place, and the broken bone is repositioned.
And then place pillows on her back, and when she wants to urinate the wool comes out easily, and she urinates, and you reduce and return it according to the manner in which you positioned it at the beginning, and then return it to its initial position.
And do this for seven days or so, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and place a reed cannula over its opening; and insert the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh, for this will reposition the fracture.
And then it should be filled with wool and remain so for several days, as we have already stated, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have described for the hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby and easily healed fractures, especially for those who study and use and understand this book of mine well; for this fracture signifies to you, in the most favorable conditions, the most direct path to its restoration and proper setting, so understand it well.
However, in the case of a broken penis in a man, take a bandage made of bird's feathers and place it over the penis; then, place a cloth wrapping over that; and let it remain wrapped for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when affected by plague.
It is convenient for someone with a fractured bone affected by plague, especially if the bone is large like the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, to bleed them and lance the area as soon as the conditions for bleeding, as we have stated above, allow you to do so.
And if the plague is causing the blood to flow, then it is necessary that you apply a cauterization to the flow, using powdered crushed glass, if no other remedy is present.
And then proceed to restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this if an abscess does not develop.
But if a serious abscess develops, then you leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days, for you will cause harmful complications.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you carefully reposition it and hold it in place with your hands with ease and little force.
But if your hands are not prepared for the application of force and the reduction of the bone with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight inches and its width is according to the size of the injury; and therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, depending on the amount needed for the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And it should be round, and of little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure upon it during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip at each end; and its top should be thick, tapering down to a point halfway from the ends.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", that is, a small spatula.
Therefore, its sharp end should be placed high on the prominent bone end.
And you push it in one motion until the bone returns, and it is secured with some binding.
Therefore, try to bring the fracture ends together, and if the broken bone end is thin and does not hold the instrument well, then you must cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grip it properly.
But if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with whatever appropriate cutting instruments we have mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible.
And then scrape away any roughness or small splinters that remain on the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and shortly after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what you deem best to greatly benefit the patient.
When your bone-setting is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and, especially if it is summer, do not put a poultice or anything oily on the wound, so that it does not cause suppuration and corruption.
And then administer styptics at the time of completing the bone setting, and leave the wound uncovered, so that you can cut the hole with scissors and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to press the wound against the fracture, for foolish doctors have often done this, causing death or a gnawing or spreading illness in their patients; that is, gangrene. And if your (treatment of) the fracture is light, soft, different from the treatment of other fractures.
If the wound is large or of poor quality, and you fear any of the bad outcomes we have described, and you find pain in the area, then you should not apply styptics there, but make a dressing of hard cloths instead of styptics and bind it tightly.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to suppurate, so that pus is generated in it, then you remove the cloth you had placed over it with wine, and then apply poultices, ointments with which it is customary to treat wounds, like tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
You should loosen the bandage and examine the wound each morning and evening until it is healed and closed, and the limb should be prepared in such a way that the pus drains from it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed or the pus has not drained from it, then know that there are complications present, so you should investigate the wound by probing it. And of those complications, those that are separable, remove and extract them; those that are not separable and cause pain in the limb, force you to cut them open, according to what is possible for you.
And if the wound has developed suppuration or festering, that is, inflammation, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply a remedy for that, appropriate to the healing of which a description precedes in its chapter.
And concerning this, which requires my instruction and your attention: when a large bone is broken, treat the limb in the same way as a broken thigh bone, or similar injuries to large limbs, and do not attempt to pull or extract it, as death frequently results from this.  Leave it undisturbed until it is able to heal, as it will often set itself within approximately 20 or 30 days.  Then treat the wound if you see fit, or leave it alone if not.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of swelling that occurs in the wake of any fracture.
Often this swelling occurs in the aftermath of a fracture's healing, especially near the joints, distorting the limb's shape and severely hindering the limb's natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent, and administer remedies that have astringent properties, such as alum, and vinegar, and myrrh, and ceruss, and similar substances.  Use some or all of these, mixing them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply this mixture to the swelling on a cloth, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage in place for several days without loosening it; then remove it and apply another, until the swelling subsides.  Or place a lead sheet gently over the area, for lead has the property of reducing inflammation in the limbs.
However, if the swelling has become hard and compacted, and removal is necessary, then carefully cut it in the highest part of the affected area, and remove the superfluous, prominent portion, or scrape it with some raspatory tool until it's gone.  Then treat the area until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural strength.
When a fractured bone is repaired, and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, this is due to various causes:  one is the excessive loosening or incorrect binding of the ligaments; or excessive tightness of the ligaments, hindering the nourishment reaching the limb; or excessive application of remedies; or improper movement at the wrong time; or insufficient blood flow to the body of the patient and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to provide the patient with nourishment and make their body soft until the blood supply is restored; and administer baths; and introduce joy and cheerfulness to them.
And afterward apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and prevent the proper operation of their function.
When, after the limb has been repaired, improper healing leads to crookedness and prominence in the broken bone and swelling, and this disfigures the shape of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not impeded from its natural function, then you should not accept the claim of anyone who believes the limb is newly broken.
But many foolish physicians and healers have done this in our land; and this practice is highly reprehensible, causing great fear, the sign of which is death.
However, if lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be soaked in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as elder leaves and their roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and an emollient plaster should be applied, such as well-prepared diachylon; or a mucilage of elder root should be made, mixed with goose fat or beeswax oil, and applied as a plaster; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar remedies known as consolidants.
And sometimes it is resolved with persistent gentle friction using damp hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all times.
However, if the crookedness has already progressed, become strong, and has turned to stone, and requires treatment with iron, then the swelling on top must be cauterized and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which is superfluous from the swelling of the bone itself should be cut away using fine scalpels, and the process must be approached with care and diligence.
Chapter 23.
Of discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from their proper place, preventing movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident results in a dislocation, it is essential to quickly attempt its reduction, and delay should be avoided; for if delayed, the area becomes inflamed and hardened, making dislocation reduction difficult.
And for this reason, the patient should not be roughly handled or stretched during the inflammation, for often this causes spasms and prolonged pain.
But when this happens, you should apply leeches to the inflamed area of the patient, and leave them until the inflammation subsides; then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and it can be reduced more easily.
And any limb thus treated will be cured, and the displaced part will return to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, following the precedent set for fractures; from the highest part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the lower jaw.
So if the two jaws are dislocated, and the dislocation is in either of two manners:  either they are displaced slightly from their place and a little softened; or they are entirely and completely dislocated, to the point that they are softened in the joint area, that the patient is drooling and cannot hold their mouth closed, and their tongue is tied up.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns on its own with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then a gentle reduction must be administered, and must not be delayed at all.
And it is necessary for the attendant to hold the patient's head, and for the physician to place the finger of one hand into the area of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he place both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hand be outside, with which he applies pressure; and he instructs the patient to move their jaws and separate them on each side; and the physician moves the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they are returned and held in place, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over those powders a cloth with a cerate, and let it be made of beeswax and oiled; and then it should be bound lightly with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to either the right or the left side; and do not force them to chew on anything; let their food be soft, until the pains have subsided and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not trouble the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, and breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fever and profuse sweating occur, and the patient's abdomen is readily agitated, and there are easy vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and often the one who suffers this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from within the chest because of its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is apparent to the touch.
And the restoration is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then compress the area with your palm with firm pressure, for it will return.
And then apply a poultice and powders to that area and press it.
But the extremity that joins the elbow and continues from it is not dislocated in any way; therefore, it must be reduced and held in place as we have said and as you have the equipment to do.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and bandages, and instruct the patient to adhere to quietude and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the reduction of muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the top part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated on the upper part of the muscle, and this is done quickly; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out on the lower parts towards the top, and more so in those whose flesh is sparse, it is easily dislocated; but in those with much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And often, men suffer injury or blows, and the muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and is swollen because it is already dislocated.
Then you must try this until you are absolutely certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts towards the armpit, so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy one, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle and, below the armpit, near the contact of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor can they move it in any way.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a simple reduction of this dislocation is made when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction is that the healer raises the patient's hand upwards; and then places their hands below the patient's armpit, and raises the joint forcibly back into its place, and the healer raises the patient's hand and extends it upwards; and then places it back down so that it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a long time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in hot water and a poultice administered that softens and smoothes, such as a mixture of the roots of marjoram, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and should be administered.
And then the patient should be placed on their back, and a wool pad should be placed under their armpit, that is neither too loose nor too tight; and then the physician places their heel on the pad, and lifts the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's arm so it is not pulled downwards, and another assistant holds the patient's head so it is not pulled downwards, so that it quickly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the affected side, and place his arm under the patient's armpit and lift the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's arm downwards from their stomach. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with them, so that it weighs them down, so that the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another way: a long, round-headed wooden post, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is placed under the patient;  then, thick cloths are placed over the top of the post, and the patient stands along the length of the post, and their arm is extended downwards on the other side; and the patient's body is pulled on the other side with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult for any of the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a wooden pole, the length of which is two arms, and the width is four fingers, and its thickness is lightened at the armpit in this shape:
And thick cloths should be tied around the rounded top so that the post does not harm the patient.
Then, place the post under the patient's armpit, and extend their arm or hand along the post downwards, and bind the post to the support, the arm, and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a ladder-like support according to its width, and the hand should be extended downwards, and the other side of the body should be suspended on the other side, for the joint will enter quickly.
And when its insertion is complete in whatever way possible, then you should place wool pads under the armpit, and then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with vinegar and egg white over the entire muscle, wrapping it around it, and then tightly bandage under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to their neck, left still, and the hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's food should be limited until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to their recovery.
Then, the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and the limb should be exercised.  Thus, if it is firm and not softened, then it is considered healed.
However, if the joint is frequently dislocated due to moisture affecting it or for another reason, then a cautery with three punctures should be administered, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage is loosened and the joint is not firmly fixed, and the plaster and bandage are removed several times, and it is not healed, and it cannot raise that arm to the parts above, then understand that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are either cut, stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be put back in its place.
For dislocations occurring near the foot and nipple or the posterior parts, reduction is achieved by pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area, and it should be treated until it is healed.
And if, after healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the thigh.
Know that the joint of the thigh is difficult to dislocate and similarly difficult to return to its proper place; and it is dislocated in all directions, especially in the front and back parts.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident under pressure or touch, whatever the specific way in which it is dislocated.  And when you align the dislocated thigh with the healthy one, this is plainly apparent to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its own muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes severely inflamed, for if it becomes severely inflamed, reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed; and especially when it is in the rear parts, for it is worse than any other type of dislocation, causing greater pain, and often results in death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce the dislocation, is that the patient's arm should be extended with both hands, and their arm outstretched.  The physician's hands should be placed on the thigh, below it, and the physician should press the joint with the fingers of their hands together, or with the heel of their palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is returned by bending the arm with the hand, until it strikes the thigh with the heel of the palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer forceful extension and a very strong effort.
And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and hold that extension, two assistants, so that it is not pulled back from the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, since it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over his hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that these things assist in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with a forceful thrust until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to place it on a compress in which there is astringency and a binding with egg white, and it should be bound with a descending constriction, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and therefore, if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not properly tightened, then you repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then loosen it.
But if there is a serious accident following the reduction of the joint, and its condition is in motion, then administer moistening in baths and gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place upon the joint a soothing, fatty substance; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is softened, then you rub the joint with moderate friction, and then repeat the soothing substance, a fatty ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the help of bathing, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a warm, moistened cow's hide over the opening and press firmly on it; and do this several times, for it is moistening and restores it to its proper condition.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of a dislocated shoulder, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated shoulder (malassen of the hand) is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy.  The opposite is true of other joints, but nonetheless speed is required in its reduction at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a painful abscess develops there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table, and the attendant extends the patient's arm, and the physician places their hand, or palm, over the protruding area of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it must be considered if the palm has already been pulled towards the front of the hand; then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table below the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is in front of the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be precisely on the table, so that the physician's hand can press on that protruding area of the joint.
And so, if it returns quickly, it is good; and if not, then grasp it firmly, avoiding the abscess, and leave it; and do not repeat this, because otherwise, neither tolerance nor success in its reduction will be possible since days will pass.
However, if the joint remains in its dislocated position, and the patient feels no cause for the problem, but that the hand is numb and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are severed or damaged, and there is no remedy for this, but the affected area should be cauterized near the point of injury, and to do this forcefully may yield some relief.
Therefore, once the injury is corrected, apply a bandage as previously described, and leave it on and firmly wrapped for five days; then, it should be removed and the patient's hand should be exercised.
But if the patient's movement is impeded and some heaviness occurs, then ease it with warm water and some rubbing several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in any direction.
And when any of those fingers are dislocated toward the palm or the underside of it, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns to its proper place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it over the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly set in its place.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is healed.
And do likewise if it is dislocated toward the underside of the hand, and bind it to that part and do your first operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do this for dislocations in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and the displaced vertebrae remain significantly out of place, then there is no cure in that case, for death is approaching the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excretion is involuntary and uncontrollable; and often some of his limbs or both legs or both arms, or one of them, are paralyzed, or a vertebra is out of place, because often it is displaced and sometimes the displacement affects four limbs.
Therefore, this has already shifted to the posterior parts and is called a sprain/dislocation (literally, "posteriority").
But the treatment for this is that you must consider if the sprain/dislocation occurred due to youthfulness, for in that case, there is no cure, nor is it a complete recovery.
But in cases of sprain/dislocation resulting from an accident, a blow, or similar causes, the ancients described detailed methods of treatment, from which the most benefit can be derived. However, I will now briefly summarise the essential points to avoid unnecessary length, as my close proximity to you and my desire to explain this clearly and concisely, using various methods that I will demonstrate, and make this clear.
And only those sprains/dislocations that occur in the back (specifically, as I will describe) can be cured.
And it is necessary that the patient be laid on their face, propped up against a wall, and be covered by a wet cloth to prevent their foot from being disturbed.
And a straight piece of wood should be placed, driven into the ground near the head of the patient's body, and another piece of wood positioned under the patient's feet on the opposite side of the wall.
And one attendant should hold the first piece of wood, and it should not be embedded too deeply; and another attendant should hold the second piece, following the same procedure.
And then wrap a cloth around the patient's head and bind a strong linen bandage firmly beneath it; and extend the end of the bandage to the piece of wood positioned near the patient's head, and tie it there; and then tightly bandage the other end of the bandage around the patient's hips, thighs, and legs; and finally secure all the bandages to the piece of wood by the patient's feet.
And then each attendant should extend the bandage to the end of the wood, and the two pieces of wood and the bandages should not be shifted from their positions, but still not too tightly, as we have described.
And the physician places their palms firmly on the spine until it returns to its proper position, or they place a table there, and then adjust the patient's feet upon the table until they return to their proper position.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is approximately three arms' lengths, and make a recess in the wall near the patient where the end of the table fits in. And then place half of the table onto the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is straightened and returns to its place.
And if you wish, turn the crank that is held by hand; and you must place two pieces of wood, each a yard long, on the ground behind the patient's head and two more behind their feet; the space between each pair of pieces should be a palm's width.  A hole should be made in each piece of wood through which the crank can turn, and each piece of wood must be firmly anchored to the ground to prevent movement.
And a round post, which the crank is on, should be turned within the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at the end of that [round post] is a hole into which the post (the crank) fits, the length of which is a palm along with what is turned; and in the other two pieces of wood is something similar to that.
And then tighten the bandages you have placed around the patient's feet in the hole at the head end [of the device], and the bandages placed around their thighs in the crank at the foot end.
And now, at each crank, stands a minister who turns the crank with their hand, and the physician applies the treatment to the spinal column as we have described.
And after the spinal column has been returned and the area treated, then it is necessary to apply a thick poultice with egg white, then linen strips; and then place over the poultice a piece of table linen whose width is about three fingers or so, and its length covers the area of the swelling, and extends over the affected part of the spinal column.
And then bind it with the bandage we have mentioned, and let the patient use subtle nourishment until healed.
And if any lingering issue remains in the area after healing, then medicines that soften and soothe must be administered via table application for a long time, as we have described; and a lead plate should be applied to that location.
And sometimes an issue arises in the back of the spinal column, caused by that dislocation; and that is the bone which protrudes, so it is not advisable to use this medicine upon it, for it will very likely cause death.
Chapter 31.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint are subject only to dislocation, and not to the kind of issues that affect other joints with minor movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inward, or outward, or forward, or backward.
And it is more often dislocated inward, and less so forward or backward.
And a sign of its dislocation inward is that when you align the patient's healthy leg with the injured one, the affected leg is longer, and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the groin, and the affected area near the groin is inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh bone has now reached there.
And the sign of dislocation outward is that the symptoms are opposite to this one.
And the sign of dislocation forward is that the patient stretches their leg to its full extent, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if they try to go forward they cannot; and their urine is retained and their groin is abscessed, and they walk on their heel.
And the sign of dislocation backward is that the knee does not extend, nor can it be bent forward as far as the groin; and the affected leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is swollen, and the head of the thigh bone is towards that place.
But the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and longstanding, having been there a while, you should administer its reduction and it will stay in its position, then that treatment isn't complete. And therefore, it's not appropriate to assume success in that instance.
But in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types, you attempt to return the joint and extend it in all directions, both inward and outward, and move it to the right and left, for a light push usually returns it and requires no further treatment.
However, if it doesn't return in this way, then you must prepare strong assistants and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either with their own hands or with a bandage tied above the knee.  Another assistant should extend the patient's hand so that they grasp their own ankles. Then, another assistant should firmly bind a bandage around the root of the thigh, and a third assistant should hold the end of the bandage.
And its extension should be either from the inside of the groin to the outside, or from the back to the spine (or buttock).
And the extension of all these must be done in one go, until the patient is lifted off the ground by his own body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common method for all four types (of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have described, it is good; and if not, then for each type, it is necessary to proceed to the appropriate treatment.
In the specific reduction of a dislocation, when the dislocation is inward, the patient should be placed on their healthy side; then apply bandages to the root of the thigh in the area between the thigh's head and the area below the groin; and then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the highest point of the pubic bone.
And then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and forcefully grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outward with vigorous extension, for this will return it to its proper place.
And this type has an easier cure, with which this limb is reduced than the other types.
However, if you are hesitant and do not comply with this type of treatment entirely, then it is necessary to tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And the length of each part should be about four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by about two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended over the top of a wooden post, and kept in this position; and the height of the patient from the ground should be about two arms' length.
And then order a strong young man to grasp the thigh, and have another young man suspend the patient, while the first young man, forcefully pulling on the thigh, will cause the joint to return to its proper place quickly.
However, in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is outwards, the patient should lie on a pillow as we have previously described in the bent-knee position; and the bandage should be tightly wrapped around the affected leg and around their foot.
And then two posts should be placed, one at their feet, and the other at their head; and then posts should be placed in the middle of the hill, covered with moistened cloths, so that the patient is not harmed by the post being between their thighs, so that the extension of the parts below is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend their part, and the physician with their hand should guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the forward parts, then it is necessary that the leg be slightly extended, and it is according to this same narrative on a hill; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected limb, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with that grasping, places the extended part below to the front part.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, but rather elevated above the ground; it is necessary that they be placed on a hard surface, just as is necessary for the extraction if their hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about their positioning on two supports; and it is on their face, and their ligaments be extended, as we have said, not much.
And it is necessary to administer compression with a table over the location where the joint is.
So, once the reduction is complete, as it should be—and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see that they are straight and the patient straightens their leg and expands it without hesitation, then know that the limb has been returned correctly—and then you bring the two thighs together and apply a compress and bandage tightly so the hip is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then remove the bandage and compress, and compare one leg with the other.
Now, if you see them straight, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then allow the patient to go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and compress that, and tighten it according to your initial tightening, and leave it for three days.
And then loosen it, and have the patient walk slowly for a few days until they are stronger.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, but not forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that when you instruct the patient to extend their leg from the thigh, then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all cases is to have the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg as far as possible; hold the patient's body close to your side, and place the patient's foot between your feet; then firmly hold the patient's knee with both hands, placing your fingers around the knee; and another assistant extends their leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that their leg adheres to the thigh with effortless ease.
Then apply a plaster and firmly bind the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove the bandage.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction by you is unnecessary, then administer strong extension with the bandages, which the narrative previously described for hip treatment.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by a slight displacement, and sometimes dislocated completely; and its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle often swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for the slight displacement is a gentle reduction, and it is that the ankle be gently stretched and held until it returns to its place.
