However, in treating a completely dislocated ankle, you must have the patient lie straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold the back of the affected leg in the middle of the leg's position.
Then you, with your right hand, take hold of the foot's upper part, and with your left hand, the lower part of the foot, at the ankle's location.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left, twice, and then push the foot with the left; and push the foot's heel with your right toward the leg, without difficulty.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot's heel against the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot is pulled back in, it is good; and if not, then repeat the procedure on it, for it returns (or has returned).
But if you are prevented from reducing it by what we have described, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tightly press a firm pad on the ground, placed between their thighs, and you have already draped a cloth over them so the patient does not get hurt.
And then the attendant holds the patient's thighs, and another attendant extends the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and then each attendant extends against the other's extension, and the pad is placed between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that the patient's body is not pulled to the lower parts, beyond its extension.
And then the physician grasps the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh at the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so, when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is evident to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with bandages.
And it is necessary to be careful of or guard against the nerves that are behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not forceful and injures it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from activity for 40 days. But if he is forced to move before that period, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if an abscess occurs, then you should apply what was previously described in our narrative, in another location for treatment and application, until it has subsided.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the foot.
The dislocated part needs to be pulled back gently without force; this is not difficult, but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in some toes of the foot, then you should have the patient place their foot on a soft, level surface, like the ground or a table, and remain positioned as if they were walking.
Then you stand, place your foot on the area above the joints, and gently press it with your foot until it returns, and you see it is now in place and no longer protruding.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot, with two ends; secure it with a proper and firm bandage for three days; and afterward, loosen the bandage and allow the patient rest and avoid walking for several days until the joint is stable and the position is secure.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations caused by blows, or fractures, or both.
When any of these causes occur, and you exert yourself in the treatment and restoration, often death results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such a case, but only those knowledgeable in the art, with long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and they should initially use medicines that soothe acute inflammations, and leave the patient to divine judgment, unless there is hope for recovery from the illness and in that you see any hope. Therefore, strive to perform the reduction as soon as possible at the onset of the condition, before it becomes a severe inflammation.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that soothes acute inflammation and treats the injury with whatever drying ointments are appropriate.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must reduce the displacement, and do in this what has been said concerning simple injuries in similar locations.
Guard and avoid, according to your ability, the dangers in the paths of fear, as my teaching precedes you; for this is better for you from your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more beneficial to your body.  This will be the case in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, of which may God be praised. Amen.
